5 Working with the Floor Plan Manual Housing  
The Floor Plan Manual Housing is designed as a workbook. It  
documents and analyzes 161 international housing projects  
built since 1947. Its compact presentation of all projects, the  
range of the projects and the long time period covered by the  
selection of examples make the Floor Plan Manual Housing a  
tool for researching the latest developments in housing, con-  
textualizing these developments through comparison with  
examples from the past 70 years. The systematic typological  
presentation of the projects allows readers to utilize the  
knowledge and ideas of others in a purposeful manner, thus  
finding inspiration for one’s own work on floor plans.  
A number of instruments in the book help provide an  
overview while offering focused approaches to reviewing  
the wealth of material. Thus, depending on one’s mood or  
particular interest, different ways of using the book are made  
possible.  
APPROACH  
A  
The chart on the inside cover or insert  
The navigation aid on the inside book cover \(soft cover  
edition\) or on the insert \(hard cover edition\) provides a  
systematic overview if you wish to search the collection of  
examples for specific floor plan ideas, access and circulation  
form, or for projects from a specific architectural practice.  
The chart categorizes the projects alphabetically from top to  
bottom by practice name and from left to right according to  
floor plan organization and means of access \(cf. essays “The  
Floor Plan Idea”, pages 48–53, and “The Path toward Access  
and Circulation”, pages 42–47\).  
APPROACH  
B  
Table of contents and introductions to typological groups  
PAGES 4–5  
The table of contents \(like the project section\) organizes  
the projects according to urban and morphological building  
types, each organized in its own chapter. Every chapter, in  
turn, is prefaced with a brief text describing the building type  
and the resultant demands for the floor plan. In this way, one  
can search and better compare the relevant projects for a  
given design task.  
APPROACH  
C  
The analytical essays  
PAGES 8–57  
The project section is preceded by six fundamental introduc-  
tory essays, whose various focal points provide the reader  
and user with different possibilities to review and analyze  
floor plans. \(see below\: “The essays as working instruments”\)  
APPROACH  
D  
Floor plan diagram overview  
PAGES 60–69  
The overview of the floor plan diagrams of all examples can  
also be used as a visual table of contents\: it allows the eye  
to travel and is intended to inspire a fresh look at the floor  
plans beyond the specific building tasks, names, or comple-  
tion dates. One of the aims of this volume is to highlight the  
surprising and inspiring elements of floor plans, which at  
first glance often go unheeded as irrelevant or uninteresting.  
6  
The project selection  
The focus of this documentation is on floor plans; therefore  
all key floor plans \(or an exemplary plan for each project\) are  
presented at the same scale of 1 \: 200, thus enabling easy  
comparison. A north indicator at the bottom of the page  
identifies the orientation.  
The projects are documented as a whole, by means of sec-  
tions, site plans, small photographs as visual supplements,  
systematic additional information in the margins, and  
written descriptions. The descriptions fulfill a dual role\: they  
are intended to guide the reader through the house or apart-  
ment; they also attempt to summarize the plan’s underlying  
idea, thus freeing the reader from the concrete floor plan  
and inspiring him or her to form a personal interpretation of  
the concept.  
The same is true for the diagrams, which in their abstrac-  
tion – that is, the standardized presentation of an exemplary  
floor plan – allow the idea of the floor plan to become more  
evident and therefore comparable to others in the book. All  
diagrams are shown as figure-ground plans. The white area  
shows the open, undefined areas to better illustrate the  
spatial flow, while the black areas denote spatial boundaries  
and predetermined zones such as bathrooms and storage  
space. All diagrams are drawn at a scale of 1 \: 500 and located  
at the top left corner of the first project page. Like a flipbook,  
they serve as a complementary search aid throughout the  
project section. Working with the Floor Plan Manual Housing  
With regard to the project plans, we have retained the  
mode of representation chosen by each architect, for the  
design idea is always reflected in the visual language of the  
presentation.  
Within each chapter, the projects are organized chrono-  
logically, in order to better trace the development of each  
building type.  
The Floor Plan Manual features only built projects, although  
an argument could undoubtedly be made that unbuilt pro-  
jects would be equally enriching for the design of floor plans.  
However, it was important that all projects "pass through  
the eye of the needle” called realization before a serious  
comparison can take place, because floor plan design is  
often subject to significant modification during the building  
process.  
Emphasis was placed on a broad international scope of the  
selected projects in order to better reflect heterogeneous,  
worldwide developments in housing.  
In addition to the objective selection criteria, a further stipu-  
lation was that each project selected is based on “exemplary  
floor plans.” In our view, an exemplary floor plan is first and  
foremost distinguished by a good or excellent utilization  
of the given situation. Secondly, and equally important, is  
that the specific idea for a floor plan should be expressed  
with the greatest clarity possible, independent of whether  
the concept could be and indeed is applicable to all or only  
a small group of users. For the most part, we have selected  
universally applicable, easily transferable solutions, although  
it also seemed justified to include, here and there, several  
designs too unique to serve as references in other settings.  
The essays as working instruments  
The introductory texts provide the reader with various op-  
tions for analysis. They demonstrate that one can only do  
justice to the complexity of what so prosaically is referred to  
as housing by layering different "ways of seeing."  
The introduction “The Sweetness of Functioning is Archi-  
tecture\: On the Use of Floor Plans” focuses on the various  
ways of reading floor plans in general, whereby reading/use  
signifies the study and development of drafting the floor  
plan on the one hand and living in or making use of the built  
plan on the other. By shedding light on both of these “levels  
of reading,” the text reveals the inherent sensuous nature of  
the seemingly abstract housing floor plan.  
The “Chronicle of Developments in Housing” documents sig-  
nificant developments in housing from the industrialization  
in the 19th century to the year 2000. Its linear, chronologi-  
cal nature allows one to retrace the multifaceted parallel  
development of historic turning points, significant paradigm  
shifts, and newly developed buildings or building types cre-  
ated as a reaction thereto or creating new impulses.  
The chronicle is complemented by a description of diverse  
“Challenges and Tendencies” in the global development of  
housing in recent years as we observe them today – without  
the benefit of hindsight.  
The focus then shifts to the organization of the structure of  
a house, the ways in which the apartments are connected to  
one another and to their surroundings\: “The Path toward  
Access and Circulation” describes the significance and poten-  
tial of the access space and analyzes different access types.  
The publisher and the editors would like to thank all those  
architects who were kind enough to search for the plans –  
and the data – of buildings long completed. They deserve  
the merit for the accomplishment of an international  
“Floor Plan Manual Housing.”  
The typology of the apartment, the search for the idea that  
underlies the specific arrangement of its individual rooms  
and their relationship to one another – be it linear, around a  
center, merging, or separate – is outlined in “The Floor Plan  
Idea.”  
While the “Floor Plan Idea” provides an analysis of built  
examples and suggests working with floor plan types as a  
design method, the essay “A Graphic Approach to Floor Plan  
Design” proposes methods of an abstract, graphic nature to  
first develop design intentions regarding spatial, functional,  
or social criteria without confining them to a concrete archi-  
tectural form too early in the process.  
The Floor Plan Manual Housing continues to be a work in  
progress. Thus we would like to once again issue an invita-  
tion to all readers and users of this manual who feel that a  
project they deem especially important – be it their own or  
a project created by someone else, old or new – is missing  
from this selection\: please contact the publisher at www.  
birkhauser.com and share your knowledge for the next  
revised edition of this work.  
7 Oliver Heckmann  
# “The Sweetness of Functioning is Architecture”\:  
# On the Use of Floor Plans  
By prefacing the term functioning with a set of sensations  
and employing the word “sweetness,” Alison and Peter  
Smithson show the work of designing a floor plan in a dif-  
ferent light. As architects who felt committed to modernism,  
they never questioned the necessity of functionality, which  
would after all be unthinkable in housing with its complex  
functional requirements. Yet they never relied solely on the  
functionality of the built structure; rather, they based their  
arguments on the pleasure of physically sensing that and  
how a house does function.  
“… the light and the space and the air are one. Sniff the  
air, sense the space, know how to act.” This expands the  
perspective\: from working on a floor plan as an abstract  
and uninhabited concept to considering what the floor plan  
will become once it is in use. And conversely, how the work  
on a floor plan can be inspired if the person who designs it  
embraces all possible facets of use\: the joy of exploring the  
potential of the floor plan, of resting in and moving through  
the space, and of engaging with the particular characteris-  
tics of a house – be it the sensory experience of its rooms  
or the manner in which they connect the inhabitant to his  
surroundings.  
Ways of Reading  
In his essay “Figures, Doors and Passages,” the architec-  
tural theorist Robin Evans also explores the relationship  
between floor plans and their use by inhabitants. To this  
end, he compares the plans for two villas, one by John  
Webb, the other by Andrea Palladio. His description focuses  
less on the floor plans as such than on two different ways  
of reading them.  
In John Webb’s design for Amesbury House in Wiltshire  
\(1661\), the use to which the house is put seems secondary,  
Ambient light, ambient air, no fuss about detail, awareness in a quiet way that the  
sweetness of functioning is architecture. … In a real building, the light and the space  
and the air are one. Sniff the air, sense the space, know how to act. How to keep this  
sense of what is going on – where the light and air is coming from, how to get in and  
out …, that is the question…  
A. + P. Smithson, in\: Changing the Art of Inhabitation, London, Munich, 1994  
John Webb\: Amesbury House, Wiltshire, 1661  
8  
for the architect has already anticipated and predetermined  
it in the plan. According to Evans, the process of industriali-  
zation and the separation of living and working led to the  
provision of privacy becoming the definitive topic in the de-  
bate on housing. The instrument of this intimacy is not the  
occupant of the house, but the house itself. Thus the plan  
for Amesbury House features a corridor and a two-tiered  
stairwell, which regulate all movement. In this case, the plan  
becomes a mechanism, capable of guiding its inhabitants to  
specific modes of behavior. One way of reading, therefore, is  
to test the spatial layout for its functionality and the system-  
atic separation of private and public zones.  
Such rules drove the design of floor plans in housing for a  
long time and, in addition to other factors, led to the extreme  
determination of floor plan structures. And this trend was  
further advanced during the modern era in particular, when  
the pursuit of economy and rationality led many architects  
Andrea Palladio\: Palazzo Antonini, Udine, 1556 “The Sweetness of Functioning is Architecture”\: On the Use of Floor Plans | Oliver Heckmann  
to such functional and apparently logical solutions. The use  
of the house has only little relevance when verifying its func-  
tionality. The functions are predetermined to such a degree  
as to eliminate any surprises in the subsequent use.  
In the second example cited by Evans, the Palazzo  
Antonini in Udine by Andrea Palladio \(1556\), the floor plan is  
only comprehensible through the history of how it was used.  
Evans uses a literary reference \(a chambermaid was asked to  
guard one of the many rooms, thus temporarily separating it  
from the spatial continuum, in order to ensure privacy for a  
tête-à-tête\) to explain that floor plans of this kind are greatly  
dependent on the ability of the occupants to control their  
boundaries. For all rooms in this plan are directly linked to  
one another as parts of an open sequence of spaces; they  
are equal in terms of structural prominence and are entirely  
indeterminate with regard to function or placement. Even  
the lavatories are connecting spaces.  
\[Architecture\] during the last two centuries … is employed more and more as a  
preventive measure; an agency for peace, security and segregation which, by its very  
nature, limits the horizon of experience – reducing noise-transmission,  
differentiating movement patterns, suppressing smells, … veiling embarrassment,  
closeting indecency and abolishing the unnecessary; incidentally reducing daily life  
to a private shadow-play. But on the other side of this definition, there is surely  
another kind of architecture that would seek to give full play to the things that have  
been so carefully masked by its antitype; an architecture arising out of the deep  
fascination that draws people towards others; an architecture that recognizes  
passion, carnality and sociality.  
“Figures, Doors and Passages,” in\: Robin Evans, Translations from Drawing to Building  
and Other Essays, London, 1997  
Riegler Riewe\: Bahnhofstraße, Graz-Straßgang, 1994  
Polyvalence  
Thus the second way of reading operates on a completely  
different level. The reader speculates what may take place  
because the floor plan does not provide any clues as to what  
should take place. The indeterminate nature of the floor plan  
of the Palazzo Antonini turns out to be polyvalent\: the poten-  
tialities of use are established, but only come to life through  
reading and using. In their residential building, Riegler Riewe  
have turned polyvalence into a design principle – not in the  
sense of Palladio, where it emerged more as a byproduct of  
his open, predominantly representative spatial order, but  
as a structured, very controlled and well-researched floor  
plan, whose numerous links between rooms of similar size  
allow for particularly diverse interpretations and functional  
allocations.  
The activities of the residents were taken into account  
here. Instead of preemptively assigning each space a specific  
function such as living room, dining room, kitchen, bedroom  
and office, bathroom and WC, shared scenarios were devised  
in which the users relax, come together or seek out privacy,  
eat, cook, sleep, work, or wash and relieve themselves. The  
degree of interconnectedness between the rooms appears  
to be the most important aspect, as if the functions of the  
floor plan can only emerge once a spatial disposition of the  
volumes has been precisely arranged. Polyvalence, in working  
on a design, means creating spaces so rich and dense that  
they can be interpreted and occupied in a variety of ways.  
Similarly, the floor plan for the Minus K House is com-  
posed of a homogenous matrix of equal-sized rooms, whose  
intended use is only hinted at through their linkages – either  
axially, on their sides, or diagonally with open corners to  
create larger areas.  
KUU\: Minus K House, Nanhui, Shanghai, 2010  
9 Peter Märkli with Gody Kühnis\: Residential Building, Trübbach, 1989  
Offer  
In Peter Märkli’s houses, functionality seems to play a subor-  
dinate role in the floor plan. He generates spatial tensions in  
his floor plan compositions and avoids anything that might  
be read as static and unambiguous; he allows the space to  
flow, creating the basis for the most varied forms of use. In  
his residential building in Trübbach, the apartments are or-  
ganized by means of three volumes set off from one another  
and the middle wall. The kitchen is inserted and forms an  
entryway together with the loggia on the other side, while  
a fireplace at the far end of the room defines yet another  
locale by virtue of its corporeality and orientation. A sanitary  
core, around which the internal paths seem to circulate, is  
set back from the facade on the other side of the middle  
wall. There is no hallway or corridor. The subtle rhythmiza-  
tion of the areas – small-large-small on the entrance side,  
and large-small-large on the other side – evokes a flow of  
movement that blurs the boundaries and functions of the  
individual rooms, a quality that is further enhanced by the  
cohesively continuous facade. Although the floor plan estab-  
lishes certain guidelines, it nevertheless remains  
polyvalent. The reader can promenade through such draw-  
ings as if on a virtual tour, measuring open spaces and  
defined areas of use, all the while speculating and deciding  
on how the house can be used.  
Even more extreme are use-neutral and semi-finished  
buildings like the Kölner Brett by the architects b&k+  
brandlhuber&kniess. The occupant is not only asked to  
establish a relationship with an existing spatial composition,  
he or she is even called upon to envision and then carry out  
its completion. This places a greater emphasis on use as an  
act of design. More than just reinterpreting the reading of a  
floor plan, here it remains to be completed.  
To begin with, the building is use-neutral and the rooms  
are empty. The drawing of the spatial configuration is no  
more than an abstract system. Spatial manipulation, a link-  
age of flat, yet wide and tall, albeit narrow spaces, options for  
horizontal and vertical connections all contribute to form a  
spatial conglomerate, which offers sufficient density in any  
imaginable constellation.  
Although the built units are empty and undefined, they  
are not devoid of atmosphere, for the concept, which is  
entirely diagrammatic to begin with, is defined by materiality  
and sensuality. Exposed concrete, massive oak planks, and  
tall wooden doors, which block the view behind the glazed  
b&k+ brandlhuber & kniess GbR\: Kölner Brett, 1999  
10 “The Sweetness of Functioning is Architecture”\: On the Use of Floor Plans | Oliver Heckmann  
Sou Fujimoto Architects\: House O, Chiba, 2007  
Alvar Aalto\: Residential building in Hansaviertel, Berlin, 1957  
facades at precisely those points where one walks through –  
all these elements define the character of the house. Given  
the emptiness and lack of definition, the occupant can seize  
upon these elements as a starting point of orientation.  
Discretion  
Evans’ comparison between the floor plans of Webb and  
Palladio leads to another design question\: was the guid-  
ing principle the separation or rather the connection of  
residents when the floor plan was being developed and its  
walls drawn? For instance, the categorical demand for the  
respect of privacy in the Amesbury House can be more subtly  
laid out in the floor plan design without such a strict spatial  
determination. An occupant who is able to connect with the  
space is also able to recognize openly formulated boundaries;  
the floor plan design may thus rely on the occupant’s  
ability for discretion. Alvar Aalto’s plan for a residential  
building in Berlin’s Hansaviertel is particularly impressive to  
study, because such a balance is achieved\: the focal point of  
the unit is clearly the idea of an open living room, – which  
he dubbed “the market square” – as the intersection of all  
movement. However, the rest of the apartment is so tact-  
fully organized that no one ever feels too exposed. One of  
the doors to the private rooms is obscured by an equally  
wide support pillar, and to reach the others one first has to  
turn a corner; the dining area is part of the living room but  
formulated as a protected niche behind a wooden screen,  
thus slightly separated.  
In House O, in which open spaces for living, eating,  
cooking, washing, and sleeping are all accommodated in a  
single large room, the branches in the floor plan point to the  
intimacy of individual areas – simply by virtue of preventing  
direct sightlines. The bed only comes into view after one has  
changed direction several times; the connection is delayed  
but not interrupted.  
The residential units of SL Court are organized as split-  
levels and it is likewise not walls, but here the subtly offset  
levels that produce a feeling of safe distance. Only the bed-  
rooms in the middle are given additional translucent sliding  
elements. One single space flows openly throughout the unit  
without completely dissolving the borders of the individual  
areas, enabling dense living in close proximity.  
b&k+ brandlhuber & kniess GbR\: Kölner Brett, 1999  
Field Design Architects\: SL Court, Urawa, 2014  
11 A. + P. Smithson\: Sugden House, Watford, 1957  
Pleasure  
In the drawings “Small Pleasures of Life,” Alison and Peter  
Smithson sketch an outline of how plain use can become  
a true pleasure. They cite the places where occupants can  
take possession of the house and explain how the house as  
a whole is enriched by the subtle definition of these places.  
Their observations seem to be rooted more in the experi-  
ences of an occupant who knows how to enjoy amenities like  
these than in the functional knowledge of an architect.  
It must be noted that the highly specific window open-  
ings, in particular, are functionally predetermined in their  
sketches\: they are arranged to direct the sightline and to  
clearly define the position of the inhabitant. There are spaces  
for sitting and looking outside, just as there are spaces for  
introspection. Rooms are not defined and delimited as din-  
ing room, bedroom, or kitchen; rather the architects extend  
an invitation to settle down in specific areas. And although  
one of the areas may be suitable for sitting on a sofa, that is  
by no means an indication that this space has been exclu-  
sively defined as a living room.  
Alison and Peter Smithson’s Sugden House is similarly  
distinguished by this kind of spatial definition of locales and  
the finely calibrated treatment of boundaries and transitions.  
This begins with the earth wall and the bench along its edge,  
which mark the ground level of the house. The house seems  
to have several entrances, giving equal value to all sides. The  
narrow metal sheets above the lintel seem to mark rather  
than shelter these entrance doors. The brickwork that sur-  
rounds the openings enhances the corporeal integrity of the  
house. In the interior, these openings inspire specific uses  
and activities; the inhabitant is connected to the outside  
world in different yet always unique ways.  
In the house, the subtle treatment of the details char-  
acterizes the atmosphere of the whole. Take, for example,  
the additional step by which the bedrooms are raised\:  
this gives them even greater privacy, while the living room  
below benefits from a higher ceiling and thus gains a  
greater spatial presence. The fireplace and the stairs define  
the large common area on the ground floor. At the same  
time, they also divide the large room and create a sense  
of shelter for the occupant rather than feeling “exposed”  
in a single large space. The bottom of the staircase is  
positioned in a manner that allows just enough space for  
lateral movement, while suggesting the path down the  
middle of the living room.  
SMALL PLEASURES OF LIFE  
to work or write at a creeper bordered window // to see the sunlight  
spread across the floor // to stand and look out without glare //  
to see the view / vegetation / trees / the ground while sitting // to see  
out from the bathroom / or perhaps be doubly enclosed // to have easy  
access to possessions without sensing their presence all the time //  
to sit comfortably and read or talk of an evening // to close wooden  
shutters in winter  
A.  
+  
P. Smithson, in\: Changing the Art of Inhabitation,  
London, Munich, 1994  
12 “The Sweetness of Functioning is Architecture”\: On the Use of Floor Plans | Oliver Heckmann  
A. + P. Smithson\: Sugden House, Watford, 1957  
Although the floor plan is far from undetermined, it  
allows for great flexibility and the house thus achieves a  
balanced relationship between predetermined function and  
possible use. The design for the Sugden House “anticipates  
the matters of living,” as Martin Steinmann\* writes. The  
house seems to be imbued with a profound knowledge of  
what makes habitation enjoyable and how use makes the  
house into a home.  
Similarly, Rudolf Olgiati’s Witzig House could have served as  
a motif for Alison and Peter Smithson’s sketches in that it is laid  
out with such a sense of comfort and scenic spaces, offering its  
inhabitants places for enjoying the surroundings. The funnel-  
shaped view from the dining area, the large window overlook-  
ing the valley, the sculptural cut-out window combined with a  
bench for taking in the mountain in one direction and facing  
the fireplace in the center of the room in the other direction\:  
these spaces alternate between opening up expansive vistas  
and appearing enclosed and introverted. Occupants naturally  
use houses of this kind in a very intuitive manner.  
Such floor plans require readers who take pleasure in  
deciphering their distinctive features. When encountering ir-  
ritations or blank spaces, the question “how is this supposed  
to function” should be an expression of curiosity rather than  
a mere commentary.  
This book addresses questions of this kind. Ultimately,  
the Floor Plan Manual Housing also provides an opportunity  
to read potential ways of living into the numerous examples  
it presents. The reader can peruse all the floor plans in this  
book, reading them simultaneously in a critical and curious, an  
analytical and imaginative manner. Even as bedtime reading.  
\* Literature  
Martin Steinmann, "Das Haus als meine Welt – Zum architektonischen Denken von  
Michael Alder" \(werk, bauen+wohnen 06, 2001\)  
Rudolf Olgiati\: Witzig House, Flims-Waldhaus, 1966  
SMALL PLEASURES OF LIFE\: to read in bed

Oliver Heckmann, Eric Zapel  
# A Chronicle of Developments in Housing  
This timeline chronicles significant developments in housing  
from the industrialization of the 19th century to the year 2000.  
The passage of time appears to be the best nexus to visualize  
concurrent historical turning points and paradigm shifts, as well  
as buildings and newly developed building types emerging as  
a reaction thereto, or with the capability to set new standards  
around the world.  
Paternalist Workers Settlements/New  
Building Type\: Row Houses  
As an alternative to the generally miserable  
living conditions in the big cities – partly  
built for philanthropic reasons, and partly  
out of the fear of workers’ riots –  
industrial workers settlements were  
realized in the immediate vicinity of  
production centers. The predominant  
housing types were row houses arranged  
in village-like layouts – often with garden  
plots to grow food to consume or sell as  
a source of supplementary income. The  
housing units in such settlements made  
a significant contribution to the develop-  
ment of the row house as a building type.  
Workers Settlements/Row House  
1850–1863  
Saltaire, Bradford \(UK\) – Titus Salt \(owner\)  
Settlement with row houses and communal facilities – built  
to the southwest of the factory to minimize exposure to  
harmful pollution.  
Workers Settlements  
1859  
Familistère, Guise \(FR\) – Jean-Baptiste André Godin  
An ensemble of three buildings in an axial formation was  
designed and also cohabited by the owner of the nearby  
factory. Besides covered courtyards, which could be used for  
community events, other amenities included a kindergarten,  
school, bathhouse, and theater. Godin’s ensemble followed  
the model of the Phalanstères as postulated by the utopian  
Charles Fourier, which had a deliberate resemblance to the  
palace at Versailles and proclaimed a cooperatively  
organized, collective vision of living and working.  
Urban Housing Shortage  
At the beginning of the 19th century, the  
industrialization led to an unprecedented  
migration of job seekers from rural regions  
to the factories in the rapidly growing cities  
in Europe and North America. Specific ur-  
ban milieus arose, with the proletariat and  
the prosperous bourgeoisie in extremely  
different housing types and locations. In  
tenements developed on a massive scale,  
miserable living conditions were often the  
norm. Berlin’s building code of 1853 had  
yet to define the apartment as an enclosed  
entity. Instead, rules determined the maxi-  
mum allowable occupancy for individual  
rooms – single rooms with shared bath-  
rooms and kitchens often accommodated  
entire families. For the most part, singles  
rented only a place to sleep.  
14 A Chronicle of Developments in Housing | Oliver Heckmann, Eric Zapel  
Housing Types for the Urban Bourgeoisie  
With the industrialization and the growth  
of cities, specific housing types arose for  
the urban bourgeoisie as well. Initially laid-  
out with palace-like suites of rooms and  
built for the upper class, more specific so-  
lutions in smaller units were developed for  
the growing middle class – with represent-  
ative sequences of rooms along the street  
and, in many cases, separately organized  
entrances and areas for servants.  
New Building Type\: Apartment House  
\(USA\)  
The row house dominated the streetscape  
of American cities until the early 19th  
century. A tenfold increase in the urban  
population between 1830 and 1870 led to  
rising land prices. Thus, despite a general  
distaste for multifamily dwellings, so-  
called “French flats” were introduced and  
by 1875 regarded as an acceptable residen-  
tial model for the middle class. Over time,  
these buildings expanded in size to cover  
entire blocks and were equipped with  
luxurious inner courtyards, penthouses and  
roof gardens in order to appeal to more  
wealthy segments of the population.  
Housing Types for the Urban Bourgeoisie  
Approx. 1870  
Casa Blocco, Via Assarotti, Genoa \(IT\)  
This building type emerged from the tradition of city palaces  
for wealthy families and was adapted to an urban,  
multi-story residential construction for the middle class.  
The two apartments per floor are laid out with similarly  
sized rooms partially offset to one another, revealing an  
almost use-neutral floor plan organization.  
Apartment House/Housing Types for the Urban Bourgeoisie  
1870  
The Stuyvesant Flats, New York \(USA\) – Richard Morris  
Hunt  
Manhattan’s first multifamily dwelling had a two-part  
circulation core – with a main staircase for residents and a  
second for servants. While the representative rooms looked  
out over the street, a long corridor led to the rear of the  
building, where narrow courtyards provided the private  
chambers with some daylight.  
Urban Housing Shortage  
1870  
Perleberger Strasse 14, Berlin \(GER\)  
Common building type for working families, with shared  
kitchens and WCs on the intermediate stair landings.  
1889  
Humane Housing Standards  
Shortly before the advent of modern  
architecture, ideas on social reform led to  
improved living standards for the working  
class throughout Europe. While in England,  
private enterprises and philanthropic  
foundations operated freely in the market  
economy, in 1889, the Société Française  
à Bon Marché in France and the German  
Cooperative Law established the pillars for  
more humane living conditions.  
Workers Settlements  
1879  
Bournville, Birmingham \(UK\) – George Cadbury \(Owner\)  
Spacious semi-detached house type in a workers village  
with recreational facilities, schools and its own train station.  
Housing Types for the Urban Bourgeoisie  
1897  
Brückenallee 4, Berlin \(GER\), Apartment Types for the  
Middle Class  
In essence, most block edge residential buildings in Berlin  
are morphologically similar, with a load-bearing middle wall  
parallel to the street facade and a front and rear building  
with side wings. They often housed residents of diverse  
economic standings under the same roof. The wealthy  
looked out over the street, low-paid workers into generally  
dark courtyards. Apartments for the middle class and  
substantially larger ones for the wealthy were equipped with  
separate circulation routes for servants – with their own  
staircases, hallways, and wings; the living areas of the  
wealthy were linked in open enfilades.  
1897  
Apartment House/Housing Types for the Urban Bourgeoisie  
1890–1930  
Chicago courtyards \(USA\)  
The organization of a U-shaped building around a courtyard  
open to the street became a typical convention for  
numerous, primarily three-story apartment buildings  
following the Great Chicago Fire in 1871. Double-loaded  
staircases are accessed directly from the landscaped  
courtyard, which serves as a semi-public transition from the  
street to the individual units.  
15 1899  
Housing Types for the Urban Bourgeoisie  
1899  
Linke Wienzeile, Vienna \(AT\) – Otto Wagner  
Residential blocks for the upper middle class,  
with servant wings along very narrow courtyards.  
Housing Types for the Urban Bourgeoisie  
Approx. 1900  
Typical Block Edge Building Berlin \(GER\)  
Apartment types for the upper class.  
Urban Housing Shortage  
Approx. 1900  
Bassena House Building Type, Vienna \(AT\)  
In Vienna, the building code permitted the development of  
85 % of a building site. The development of the Bassena  
House – named after a washbasin located in the hallway –  
at least provided each one-room family unit with its own  
kitchen in an anteroom.  
16  
Humane Housing Standards  
1902-08  
Herne Hill Peabody Estate, London \(UK\) – Darbishire,  
Wallis, Wilkens  
Developed as an inner-city housing type for working  
families, five 1–3-room apartments with shared bathrooms  
and washing rooms on each floor form a rational and  
cost-saving spatial principle, which was implemented in  
numerous residential buildings by the Peabody Trust over  
the following 40 years.  
Housing Types for the Urban Bourgeoisie  
1903  
Rue Franklin, Paris \(FR\) – Auguste Perret  
One of the first residential buildings erected in reinforced  
concrete. The living areas are generously linked to form an  
enfilade, the kitchen and servant staircases remain  
separate. Above the commercial ground floor, the building is  
U-shaped in order to bring in more natural light.  
1902  
Garden Cities of To-morrow, Ebenezer  
Howard  
In light of miserable urban living condi-  
tions, a visionary idea designed to create a  
symbiosis between the advantages of rural  
and urban life. In a scheme of concentric  
rings, garden cities surround a central city  
beyond a green belt. The population is  
limited – if exceeded, further garden cities  
supplement the urban group. This influen-  
tial model was implemented only in terms  
of typology \(and drastically changed\),  
while the economic and social strategies –  
simple building methods, collective forms  
of ownership, and the prohibition of land  
speculation – slipped into obscurity. The  
concept was influential above all in the  
later development of New Towns as well as  
suburban, single-family house settlements.  
Garden Cities/Row House  
1906  
Hellerau Garden City \(GER\) – Riemerschmid, Muthesius,  
Tessenow  
Muthesius’ design for a 5.40 m-wide timber framed row  
house type with three rooms, an eat-in kitchen and annex  
with WC, stall, and laundry room. A Chronicle of Developments in Housing | Oliver Heckmann, Eric Zapel  
City Expansions  
Large-scale city expansions helped allevi-  
ate the housing shortages. Influenced by  
contemporary social debates, considerable  
improvements in living standards were  
followed, such as the provision of public  
parks and community facilities as well  
as more modern apartments – spacious  
layouts for families and units specifically  
designed for single households – with  
much consideration given to the quality of  
light and air circulation.  
City Expansions  
1917–1925  
Amsterdam Zuid \(NL\) – H. P. Berlage  
Berlage’s second master plan for the expansion of  
Amsterdam placed important public spaces and buildings  
as accents at the junctures of the main axes flanked by long  
residential blocks. In order to achieve a mixed balance of  
residents, diverse apartment types were created – including  
minimal apartments for singles – in the slim, individually  
articulated buildings with large interior courtyards.  
Apartment House/Housing Types for the Urban Bourgeoisie  
1907  
Belnord Apartments, New York \(USA\) – Hiss and Weekes  
Occupying an entire city block, a massive cubic volume is  
organized around a spacious semi-public courtyard with a  
landscaped garden inspired by European models. The  
apartments are divided down the middle by a generous  
hallway space\: representative living rooms \(and servants’  
quarters\) are oriented toward the loud street, the sleeping  
quarters toward the courtyard.  
Garden Cities/Row House  
1908  
Letchworth Garden City \(UK\) – Unwin & Parker  
A self-sufficient estate incorporating areas for farming,  
industry, a park and a residential quarter laid out in a  
star-shaped formation. The diverse row house types are  
remarkable for their shallow depth and dually oriented rooms.  
Urban Housing Shortage  
1906  
National Hotel, San Francisco \(USA\)  
“Rooming houses” were developed to fill a niche in the  
real-estate market\: cell-like units are organized around light  
and air shafts and share a bathroom. In these buildings  
intended to house temporary renters, common shared  
spaces were reduced to an absolute minimum in order to  
maximize profits.  
Affordable Housing  
After World War I, economic crises and  
egregious housing shortages in Europe led  
to the vigorous pursuit of cost-effective  
mass housing solutions. The ration-  
alization of production methods and a  
principally economic view of floor plan  
organization, building types, and their  
ensembles with the aim of standardization  
governed the debate on housing for many  
years to come.  
Affordable Housing  
1918  
“On Economical Building\: A Contribution on Housing  
Estates” – Peter Behrens, H. de Vries  
This comprehensive study addresses, among other things,  
grouped buildings and back-to-back housing typologies in  
order to more effectively utilize building parcels. It presents  
strategies for typification and rationalization and  
encourages the use of affordable materials like concrete.  
1918  
17 City Expansions  
1919–22  
Justus Van Effencomplex, Rotterdam-Spangen \(NL\) –  
Michiel Brinkman  
As part of the development of Spangen Polder under the  
direction of city-architect J. J. P. Oud, where – inspired by  
Berlage – closed, mid-height block edge buildings were  
preferred over the garden city typology, here a symbiotic  
model was sought after. An urban block edge development  
encloses common courtyards with separate programs. The  
single-floor apartments on the ground and 2nd floors are  
accessed directly from the courtyard level, while the  
prominent, all-encompassing, and almost street-wide  
galleries on the third floor provide a second stage for  
neighborly interaction and access to the maisonette units.  
City Expansions  
1923  
Hornbækhus, Copenhagen \(DK\) – Kay Fisker  
A “reform block” with roughly 300 apartments in an urban  
expansion zone surrounds a spacious, green courtyard. The  
homogeneity of its exterior facade lends it a monumental  
character. The living rooms face the street, while bedrooms  
and the kitchen – directly linked to the garden below by a  
second staircase – overlook the interior of the block.  
1919  
Affordable Housing/Row House  
1920  
“House with One Wall,” Vienna \(AT\) – Adolf Loos  
Loos patented his construction for this economic row  
house type\: only the dividing walls between houses  
were bricked, the facades are made of wood.  
Row House  
1925  
Cité Moderne, Brussels \(BE\) – Victor Bourgeois  
The design enriches the row house typology with a jagged  
row house development set at a 45° angle. Partially linked in  
their corners, rooms surround all sides of a central hall with  
staircase – day rooms as well as the kitchen and WC on the  
ground floor, bedrooms and bathrooms on the floor above.  
Affordable Housing/Garden City  
1924–28  
Sunnyside Gardens, New York \(USA\) – Clarence S. Stein,  
Henry Wright  
Affordable housing development with simple flats and  
duplexes for families is a predecessor of garden cities in the  
USA. The layout of the estate adheres to the existing block  
structure, however, by not parceling out the individual lots,  
an open ensemble with offset buildings emerges where  
green spaces in front of and behind the houses connect to  
one another.  
18  
City Expansions  
1925  
Hufeisensiedlung, Berlin-Britz \(GER\) – Bruno Taut  
One of 17 large housing developments built in Berlin  
between 1925 and 1933 with an intentional humanistic  
approach to modern architecture, this project saw the  
combination of single-story flats in the “horseshoe” with  
row houses. At the time, the flats were of ample size \(living  
and dining room 24 m², kitchen 10 m², master bedroom 20  
m²\) and each with its own garden plot in the yard of the  
horseshoe.  
Affordable Housing  
1925  
Multifamily Dwelling – Ludwig Hilberseimer  
Rational floor plan with combined kitchen and bath, living  
room as circulation space, and cabin-like rooms.  
Row House  
1925  
Kiefhoek Row Houses, Rotterdam \(NL\) – J. J. Oud  
Extremely compact, 4.20 m-wide house type for six people  
with minimal circulation space and no bathroom. A Chronicle of Developments in Housing | Oliver Heckmann, Eric Zapel  
Building Systems  
The development of innovative building  
elements, new concepts of prefabrica-  
tion, and efficient, systematic building  
processes was influenced by many factors\:  
massive housing shortages, a fascination  
with technical innovation such as Ford’s  
new automobile production methods, new  
steel construction methods in American  
high-rises and industrial buildings, as  
well as the search for a rational aesthetic.  
According to Ernst May, the reduction  
in shape and size of building parts was  
intended to “have a beneficial impact on  
the simplicity and integrity of architectural  
design.”  
Building Systems/Affordable Housing  
1926–30  
Frankfurt Slab System, Floor Plans for the Praunheim  
Slab Type \(GER\) – Ernst May  
Over 1,000 houses were built in Frankfurt using such  
industrialized assembly methods. The serial production of  
concrete building elements and the introduction of modern  
building methods were aimed at reducing building times  
and optimizing the construction of housing, so that even  
residents with modest incomes could manage to acquire  
affordable living space. The ground floor plan in Praunheim  
– part of the “first test group in Frankfurt” – followed a  
principle similar to all residential buildings in Frankfurt’s  
housing program, in which a spacious living room is  
separated from a small, standardized kitchen outfitted with  
built-in furniture and equipment designed by Margarete  
Schütte-Lihotzky.  
City Expansions  
1927–30  
Karl Marx Hof, Vienna \(AT\) – Karl Ehn  
As an urban manifest of social housing, the blocks of this  
estate had large common courtyards with communal  
facilities such as laundry rooms, bathhouses, a library,  
shops, and restaurants. While the exterior is almost  
fortress-like in nature, the apartments are very compact and  
for the most part oriented in only one direction.  
1927  
Building Exhibitions and Model Estates  
Starting in the 1920s and initiated by  
the “Deutscher Werkbund “ \(German  
Association of Craftsmen\), among others,  
building exhibitions became testimonials  
to the “Neues Bauen” movement, which in-  
troduced Modernist architectural concepts  
– until then only discussed in international  
avant-garde circles – to the wider public.  
Together with model estates, they became  
an instrument of planning and building  
culture, which with often uniform concepts  
and projects created impulses for aes-  
thetic, technical, and social changes.  
Building Exhibitions and Model Estates  
1927  
Building Exhibition Weißenhofsiedlung Stuttgart \(GER\)  
– Ludwig Mies van der Rohe \(master plan\)  
In contrast to more established conventions of the time, the  
master plan called for a layout of freestanding buildings  
with flat roofs, following the topography of the site.  
Building Exhibitions and Model Estates  
1927  
Weißenhofsiedlung Housing \(GER\) – Ludwig Mies van der  
Rohe  
The steel-frame construction and the neutrality of the  
facades enable flexible floor plans arranged with movable  
walls located between the load-bearing columns to create  
space for four to six inhabitants. Possible configurations  
include cabin plans with the living room as circulation  
center.  
Building Exhibitions and Model Estates/Row House  
1926  
Bauhaus-Siedlung Dessau-Törten \(GER\) – Walter Gropius  
\(master plan, row houses\)  
The row house types of this experimental estate project –  
on large parcels of land with enough room for keeping small  
animals or a vegetable garden – were built using serial  
construction processes and standardization methods and  
were intended as inexpensive housing for working families.  
An increase in building costs led to a further simplification  
of the later realized type “sietö IV”\: the split-level principle  
reduced the circulation space, baths were taken in the  
laundry room.  
Building Exhibitions and Model Estates/Row houses  
1927  
Row houses, Weißenhofsiedlung Stuttgart \(GER\) –  
J. J. P. Oud  
Housing type with low-lying annex for utility rooms on the  
stair landing, additional entrances from the rear yard, and a  
separate WC and bathroom \(as connecting room\).  
19 1928  
1928  
Floor Plan Studies – Alexander Klein  
Symptomatic of Modernist pursuits of  
systematically optimized housing types,  
such studies formed an important basis  
for their standardization. After extensive  
analyses of functional processes, move-  
ment flows, the placement of furniture,  
lighting conditions, and so on, Klein  
postulated a clear division of rooms into  
three groups – living, sleeping and utility  
– whose individual functions could in the  
best case be carried out with as little over-  
lap as possible. The “Room Group” floor  
plan was presented in 1928 at the “Home  
and Technology” exhibition in Munich.  
New Building Type\: Residential  
High-rises  
The high-rise was developed in the USA  
in reaction to rising inner-city land prices  
following the Civil War. Made possible by  
innovations in steel-frame construction  
and the advent of new safety measures for  
elevators, they were initially conceived for  
commercial purposes. Around the turn of  
the century, residential high-rises emerged  
in a similar style. In part conceived as  
residential hotels to circumvent height  
restrictions for purely residential buildings,  
their shapes were for the most part derived  
from the lot structure and zoning laws.  
Starting in the late 1920s, varying models  
for different income groups led to the  
creation of both luxurious residences for  
the rich as well as inner-city housing for  
the middle class.  
20  
Floor Plan Studies  
1928  
Apartment for the Working Woman \(GER\), Margarete  
Schütte-Lihotzky  
Apartment type presented at the “Home and Technology”  
exhibition in Munich.  
Garden Cities  
1929  
Radburn Estate \(USA\) – Clarence S. Stein, Henry Wright  
Howard’s Garden City concept served as a role model for the  
standardized, two-story detached homes – each with a  
garage, kitchen, living and dining room on the first floor and  
bedrooms on the second floor – arranged in groups around  
cul-de-sacs that flank a large public park. Semi-public paths  
connect the streets to this green space.  
Building Exhibitions and Model Estates/Row House  
1929  
Dammerstock Estate Row Houses, Karlsruhe \(GER\) –  
Walter Gropius  
Parallel slabs in a strict north-south orientation built  
according to “rational building methods.” Half of the  
housing units are only 5.60 m-wide row houses, with  
transverse central staircases as organizing elements.  
Residential High-rises  
1928  
Woodstock Tower, New York \(USA\) – H. Douglas Ives  
Part of the world’s first high-rise estate, Tudor City – a  
privately financed, speculative development aimed at  
offering office workers accommodation close to their place  
of employment – the building consists of over 400  
hotel-like 1-room apartments \(“Efficiencies”\) on its lower  
floors. More spacious apartments are located on the upper,  
terraced levels.  
Residential High-rises  
1929  
San Remo Tower, New York \(USA\) – Emery Roth  
The highest of Emery Roth’s luxury high-rises on Central Park  
is comprised of two towers on a U-shaped, slightly terraced  
base covering the width of a city block. The maisonettes in  
the towers – with representative rooms below and sleeping  
quarters for residents and servants above – offer their  
inhabitants views in every direction and the feeling of living  
in a freestanding villa high above the city.  
Residential High-rises  
1929  
1420 Lake Shore Drive, Chicago \(USA\) – Hooper & Janusch  
9- and 11-room single-floor apartments and 16-room  
maisonettes are equipped with high-quality finishes, open  
fireplaces, and the latest innovations such as refrigerators.  
The floor plans exhibit a typical layout for the time period\:  
large, representative rooms look out over Lake Michigan and  
are connected by a long corridor past the servants’ quarters  
to the bedrooms at the rear. A Chronicle of Developments in Housing | Oliver Heckmann, Eric Zapel  
1929  
Housing for the Minimum Subsistence  
Level  
In 1929, an international group of archi-  
tects under the direction of Ernst May  
posed the question of how to design an  
apartment for the minimum subsistence  
level at the second Congrès International  
d’Architecture Moderne \(CIAM\). 105 designs  
in a consistent graphic presentation with  
comparative values like apartment size,  
volume, and window surface area were  
collected to form a systematic floor plan  
typology for affordable housing.  
Housing for the Minimum Subsistence Level  
1929  
Apartment types Frankfurt a. M./Budapest  
Housing for the Minimum Subsistence Level/Slab Housing  
1930  
Cabin plan, Kassel \(GER\) – Otto Haesler  
Small individual rooms accessed through the living room.  
Residential High-rises/City Expansions  
1930–34  
Les Gratte-Ciel de Villeurbane, Lyon \(FR\) – Môrice Leroux  
Two long slabs, repeatedly interrupted by perpendicular,  
terraced residential towers, were created as part of a larger  
master plan for a new urban district. Various apartment  
sizes – but predominantly 3-room units – are efficiently  
designed to reduce the rent for the working-class residents.  
The high density of the configuration is moderated by  
setback roof terraces, public plazas, and a number of shops  
on the ground floor.  
New Building Type\: Slab Housing –  
Walter Gropius  
Of the opinion that architecture should  
be practical and standardizable, Gropius  
established rules for a building type that  
was in part motivated by medical findings  
in the late 19th century concerning the  
effects of sunlight in preventing the spread  
of hazardous bacteria and the necessity of  
proper ventilation for healthy housing. The  
use of light studies resulted in precisely  
calculated distances between the parallel,  
open layout of the slab buildings oriented  
to the east and west. This configura-  
tion became an important instrument  
in modern urban planning. Although the  
scheme was implemented in row house  
developments as well, the dominant image  
of slab housing stems from multi-story,  
freestanding, linear buildings with shared  
access and circulation, be they vertical or  
horizontal.  
Slab Housing  
1930  
Narkomfin, Moscow \(USSR\) – Ginzburg & Milnus  
A new model of society was proclaimed with the Russian  
Revolution, in which the traditional role of the family was  
questioned in favor of the collective. To facilitate the  
employment of women, kindergartens and canteens were  
incorporated into the building, while the private kitchens in  
the compact, split-level and duplex units of the residential  
slab were minimized. All collective spaces are  
accommodated in a separate building.  
Slab housing/City Expansion  
1930  
Siemensstadt, Berlin-Jungfernheide \(GER\) – Hans Scharoun  
\(master plan\), Hans Scharoun, Walter Gropius, Hugo Häring  
\(apartment types\)  
A strict east-west oriented layout of slab buildings for  
employees of the Siemens production facilities. Double-loaded  
staircases provide access to the apartments. In Scharoun’s  
design \(upper left\), a living room spanning the width of the  
building – with a loggia, dining niche, and windows with flower  
boxes – serves as the central circulation space, from which a  
“slipper hall” leads to a group of bedrooms.  
1930  
21 1933–35  
Highpoint Terrace, London \(UK\) – Lubetkin and Tecton  
One the first Modernist residential buildings in England –  
where a ground floor raised on pilotis with a tea room and  
winter garden extends an invitation to linger – was  
commissioned by the industrialist Sigmund Gestetner for  
his office workers. The single-floor flats are accessed from a  
meandering circulation area. Due to the building’s  
double-cross shape, most of the orthogonally organized  
units are exposed to natural light on three sides.  
1935  
Casa Rustici, Milan \(IT\) – G. Terragni, P. Lingeri  
Modern architecture integrated into a historic block  
structure\: two slab-like building segments, which – opti-  
mally cross-ventilated and oriented east-west – frame a  
courtyard that opens up to the city, are linked by a layer of  
balconies along the street to complete the block infill type.  
A spacious hall serves as the circulation center; almost all  
important living areas look out over the urban avenue.  
Building Exhibitions and Model Estates/Row House  
1932  
Werkbundsiedlung Vienna Row House \(AT\) – Adolf  
Loos  
The “Raumplan” concept was adapted to this  
three-story row house\: a double-height living room is  
bordered by a gallery with a built-in sofa.  
1935  
Kollektivhus Stockholm \(SE\) – Sven Markelius  
A residential building conceived to unburden working  
mothers. There is a day-care facility for children, meals can  
be ordered from the central kitchen and delivered to most  
apartments by dumbwaiter, a laundry chute leads straight  
to the serviced laundry room. Storage for sporting  
equipment, a restaurant, a reading room, and the roof  
terrace are all shared. Alternatively, small yet efficiently  
laid-out kitchens in each unit offer residents the possibility  
to cook for themselves. All ancillary rooms and bedrooms  
branch off from the open living room.  
1931  
Building Systems  
1931–34  
Cité de la Muette, Drancy near Paris \(FR\) – Beaudouin,  
Lods, Mopin, Bodiansky  
This project is regarded as the first systematically  
designed and erected large-scale housing estate, which  
– following the urban design principles of CIAM – merged  
industrially planned manufacturing cycles with customized  
construction methods. The distance between the  
steel-frame building structures was determined by the  
dimensions of the reinforced concrete facade elements  
produced on-site. Lightweight facade components were  
produced in the workshops of Jean Prouvé.  
Building Systems/Slab Housing  
1934  
Bergpolder Residential High-rise, Rotterdam \(NL\)  
– Van Tijen, Brinkman & Van der Vlugt  
An experimental residential high-rise realized as a  
steel-frame structure with wooden floors and a panel  
facade, oriented north-south to optimize the lighting  
situation of the living spaces. The efficient floor plan  
design aimed to minimize circulation areas; a sliding  
door enables flexible use of the rooms adjacent to  
the living room. The elevator stopped only at every  
second floor.  
Building Systems  
1935  
Experimental House, Göteborg-Kallebeck \(SE\) – Erik  
Friberger  
The only fixed elements are the reinforced concrete  
structure and the access and adjacent sanitary cores. The  
final layouts of the units were completed in their entirety  
by the residents.  
22 A Chronicle of Developments in Housing | Oliver Heckmann, Eric Zapel  
Housing in the Post-war Era  
After the Second World War, many cities  
experienced an acute housing crisis.  
Housing developments therefore increas-  
ingly drew on the already established  
urban design and architectural repertoire  
of the Modernists – often at larger scales  
than before due to the housing shortage  
and at times built to inferior standards.  
Reconstruction efforts primarily followed  
the tenets of the landscaped automo-  
tive city, for which even completely intact  
urban quarters were razed. Traffic arteries  
linking growing suburban developments to  
the city exacerbated the situation.  
Housing in the Post-war Era  
1943–49  
Stuyvesant Town, New York \(USA\) – Irwin Clavan, Gilmore  
Clarke  
Linked rows of austere, cross-shaped towers replaced 18  
impoverished city blocks. Initially conceived as mass  
housing for war veterans, this privately financed large-scale  
estate provided its residents with affordable housing in  
park-like surroundings in the middle of the city.  
Housing in the Post-war Era  
1945–1964  
Reconstruction of Le Havre’s City Center \(FR\) – Auguste  
Perret  
The layout of the historic city center was readapted for the  
reconstruction and rebuilt with a uniform, representative  
building ensemble realized using methods taken from  
industrial mass housing. In the 4-room apartments, the  
living room forms the organizational and spatial focal point\:  
work or dining areas can be flexibly connected along the  
street facade, bedrooms adjoin directly in the lateral axis.  
The – very innovative – mechanically ventilated bathrooms  
are accessed from three sides.  
Workers Settlements  
1936–54  
Sunila Workers Settlement, Kotka \(FI\) – Alvar Aalto  
In this ensemble constructed over multiple decades, rows of  
three-story units are arranged along the southern slopes,  
gardens, and transport arteries of a valley. The building  
organization takes advantage of the topographical situation\:  
1-room apartments on the lower level are accessed from the  
south side, the 2-room apartments above either directly  
from the ground floor or by stairs from the north. The idea  
to provide each apartment with its own entrance instead of  
sharing a single staircase was based on sociological factors\:  
privacy and identity of place despite high density was an  
important concern of the architect.  
Urbanization Processes in Latin America,  
North Africa, and Asia  
Concurrent with the developments in  
Europe and North America, many cities in  
North Africa, Latin America, and Asia were  
experiencing large population increases.  
This was partly due to the influx of political  
refugees, but above all else to large-scale  
economic migration to the urban centers.  
As the existing structures were not able to  
cope, large informal settlements arose, as  
did new housing policies and methods of  
dealing with the situation. Still under colo-  
nial rule, large-scale housing estates were  
initiated in North Africa as testing grounds  
for similarly large developments in France.  
Urbanization Processes in Latin America  
1945  
El Silencio Residential Estate, Caracas \(VE\) – Carlos Raúl  
Villanueva  
An urban renewal ensemble of housing blocks for residents  
with low incomes was erected to replace a rundown  
district. The apartments in Block 4 have three to four  
similarly sized linked rooms, which generate a nearly  
use-neutral floor plan open to interpretation.  
Urbanization Processes in North Africa  
1945–48  
New Gourna Village, Luxor \(EG\) – Hassan Fathy  
Built to prevent expanding informal structures from  
encroaching on a historic burial site, the design for this  
settlement takes its cue from traditional social  
communities comprised of 10 to 20 families, each with its  
own designated area. The housing units are arranged  
around small courtyards. Utility rooms occupy the ground  
floor, bedrooms connect to a loggia on the upper floor that  
doubles as an outdoor living room.  
1945  
23 Housing in the Post-war Era  
1946-62  
Churchill Gardens, London \(UK\) – Powell and Moya  
In this publicly funded residential estate, which replaced an  
urban neighborhood badly damaged during the war, a wide  
spectrum of housing types were on offer. In the tall slab  
buildings, external staircases lead to single-story flats with  
open kitchens and open galleries to stacked maisonettes.  
1946  
New Towns Act \(UK\)  
This program initiated the development in  
Great Britain of 27 new, autonomous cities  
over the coming 50 years. It was conceived  
in reaction to the acute housing shortage  
after the war and also to avoid further  
urban sprawl in the larger metropolitan  
regions – particularly London. New Towns  
were initially a modern interpretation of  
the garden city, but in the end were often  
realized as automotive cities. As a model,  
they were adopted worldwide.  
24  
Suburbia  
Following the war, the erection of de-  
tached single-family home developments  
outside the city dramatically changed the  
face of domestic culture in the USA in only  
25 years. Construction programs such as  
the Housing Act of 1949, the expansion of  
expressways, and new serial production  
methods coalesced in a building boom,  
which first and foremost made it possible  
for the middle class to move out of the  
increasingly unpopular, large cities. Despite  
contributing greatly to the overdevelop-  
ment of rural landscapes, the suburbs  
became a symbol of American lifestyle in  
the post-war era – and were copied around  
the world.  
Suburbia  
1947–51  
Levittown, Long Island \(USA\) – Levitt & Sons  
This is the first of more than 200 developments named after the  
proprietor and project developer. Designed and built by the  
same company, they optimized planning and construction  
processes ensuring short construction times. The residents  
could pick from a selection of catalogue models. The entrance of  
the housing types with unfinished but upgradable attics often  
led directly into an open living area and kitchen. In the one-story  
types with upgradable attics, the sleeping quarters were located  
to one side, in the two-story types on the floor above.  
New Towns  
1947  
Harlow \(UK\) – Frederick Gibberd  
40 km outside London and built in a hilly, wooded, and  
sparsely populated landscape to alleviate the strain on  
industrial areas in the east of the metropolis, Harlow is  
divided into four districts, each conceived for 20,000  
residents and 3 km in radius, which in turn are composed of  
multiple neighborhoods grouped around their individual  
central areas.  
Housing in the Post-war Era/New Towns  
1949–54  
Tiburtino Residential Quarter, Rome \(IT\)  
A modern urban expansion combined with the  
heterogeneous nature of village-like structures and vernacu-  
lar architecture. Residential high-rises, terraced slabs, and  
row houses form a composition that follows the course of  
the streets. In the high-rises, the living room with loggia acts  
as a central circulation space, while in the lower slabs a  
corridor or hall divides the living and dining areas from the  
bedrooms. Offset and staggered building volumes, stepped  
access galleries, balconies, and rooftop gardens enliven the  
streetscape of this planned district. A Chronicle of Developments in Housing | Oliver Heckmann, Eric Zapel  
Housing in the Post-war Era  
1952  
Unité d'Habitation, Marseille \(FR\) – Le Corbusier  
The Unité d‘Habitation was conceptualized as an  
autonomous slice of city for 1,600 residents – the number  
Le Corbusier deemed necessary to generate a functioning  
urban community – with shopping streets, shared rooftop  
spaces as well as the “Rues Intérieures” on every third floor,  
from which different apartment types are accessed from  
both sides. The distinctive, very narrow maisonettes run the  
entire width of the building above and below the access  
floors. \(see page 184\)  
Urbanization Processes in Latin America  
1950–52  
Pedregulho, Rio de Janeiro \(BR\) – Affonso Eduardo Reidy  
This estate in an open landscaped park is regarded as an  
important example of Brazilian post-war Modernist social  
housing. The most prominent building is the curvilinear slab  
resting on pilotis that snakes its way through the topography  
of the site. Its mid-section remains open to the elements, is  
accessed from the upper slope and provides space for public  
programs such as a kindergarten or playground. The lower  
levels mostly contain very compact flats. Despite their  
modest size, the two levels of maisonettes above are visually  
linked by a connecting void between the floors.  
Housing in the Post-war Era  
1952–60  
Stalinallee, Berlin \(GER\) – Hermann Henselmann \(master  
plan, high-rise\)  
As in other European capitals of the former Eastern Bloc,  
monumental, representative architecture as an expression  
of political power was adopted for public housing.  
“Residential palaces for workers” with roughly 3,000  
apartments in blocks and towers offered many comforts to  
their inhabitants – such as high ceilings, spacious floor  
plans, and technological innovations including two-way  
intercom systems, telephone lines, and garbage chutes –  
which social housing in the West was not in a position to  
provide. \(see page 229\)  
Urbanization Processes in North Africa  
1954  
ATBAT Collective Housing, Casablanca \(MA\) – Woods,  
Bodiansky, Candilis  
As part of an estate for former slum inhabitants, three  
multi-story structures are based on the traditional courtyard  
houses of the region. The 2-room apartments in the slab  
building are arranged along patios that alternate every floor  
from one facade to the other. Completely walled-in but  
open to the sky, they form a climatic and social buffer by  
separating the interior rooms from the external access  
galleries and shielding them from direct sunlight. The  
apartments are cross-ventilated and the kitchen – a  
potential heat source – is located outside.  
Slab housing  
1952  
Flats for Air France, Brazzaville \(CG\) – P. B. Berruet  
Behind the adjustable brise-soleil, enclosed areas for the  
staircases, kitchens, and bathrooms alternate with living  
and sleeping areas open to both sides. By placing the  
circulation path along the facades, a buffer to the set-back  
living areas is formed, protecting them from direct sunlight  
and the tropical climate.  
Housing in the Post-war Era  
1954  
Torre Velasca, Milan \(IT\) – BBPR  
During the post-war reconstruction period, the  
multifunctional high-rise with commercial space on the  
ground floor, 18 office floors and eight residential levels at  
the very top strove to increase the density of the  
pre-existing city instead of replacing it. This position was  
pursuant to the contemporary debate in Italy, which  
endeavored to develop modern architecture in a contextual  
dialogue with the historic city instead of in opposition to it.  
A range of different apartment types was produced\: studios,  
flats with spacious, corner living rooms, as well as  
maisonettes.  
Urbanization Processes in North Africa  
1954  
Sidi Othman Apartments, Casablanca \(MA\) – André  
Studer  
The slabs and high-rises are inspired by North African  
housing types. The compact units are organized around  
large living rooms and cantilevered patios. Stacked in an  
offset manner and each supported by a single column,  
these patios lend the facade its distinctive character.  
1954  
25 1954  
New Building Type\: Low Rise High  
Density  
Criticism of urban sprawl due to the con-  
struction of detached single-family homes  
led to a search for alternative solutions.  
In contrast to other multifamily housing  
schemes, each unit was to be provided  
with private, outdoor spaces and a direct,  
preferably ground-level entrance from  
the public sphere, while interlocking or  
semi-detached constellations of similar,  
neighboring units simultaneously ensured  
a high ratio of land use. Besides more  
classical row house configurations, low-  
lying estates with cluster-like structures  
emerged that took advantage of back-to-  
back arrangements, patchwork-like pat-  
terns, or more complex, three-dimensional  
groupings. Lighting recesses in the building  
volume such as patios became distinctive  
design features.  
Mass Housing in Asia  
In Asian cities like Singapore and Hong  
Kong, or in countries like South Korea,  
“emergency” housing types were initially  
implemented to deal with issues such as  
uncontrollable growth and the resulting in-  
formal settlements. From such immediate  
challenges, residential property developers  
arose who have since dominated large seg-  
ments of housing construction. The supply  
of building land was partially monopolized  
and large-scale development given priority,  
as providing housing to the population  
became an important aspect of political  
and economic stabilization policies. Large  
housing estates were built not only in the  
form of New Towns, but also in part as  
replacements for older, more organically  
evolved, low-rise urban districts.  
26  
Low Rise High Density  
1955  
Halen Estate, Herrenschwanden \(CH\) – Atelier 5  
In this dense ensemble on sloping terrain with very deep,  
primarily 4 m-wide, multi-storied housing units, the upper  
rows have gardens on the roofs of the unit below. Each  
house enjoys an unobstructed view. \(see page 298\)  
Mass Housing in Asia  
1954  
Shek Kip Mei Estate, Hong Kong \(HK\) – Resettlement  
Department  
On Christmas day, 1953, a fire destroyed vast parts of an  
informal settlement in Shek Kip Mei inhabited by a large  
number of refugees from the Chinese Civil War. A relocation  
authority was initiated, marking the beginnings of a  
program of heavily subsidized housing construction under  
the later established Hong Kong Housing Authority. Initially,  
only emergency housing was built, but with improved  
political and economic stability, more permanent structures  
and increased standards for large portions of the urban  
population were introduced. The H-shaped block type  
realized in Shek Kip Mei in 1954 provided every family with  
only one room. Accessed by wraparound external galleries,  
they were arranged back-to-back, with the result that  
perforated dividing walls were necessary for cross-ventila-  
tion. Kitchens and bathrooms were shared.  
Housing in the Post-war Era  
1955–83  
Barbican Estate, London \(UK\) – Chamberlin, Powell, Bon,  
Arup  
By rebuilding a site that had been vacant since the end of  
the war, an attempt was made to reinvigorate inner-city  
housing. The large-scale project offered the possibility of  
creating a unique ensemble by combining cultural and  
educational institutions as well as a variety of building types  
around generous outdoor green spaces. With over 100  
different floor plans, a wide range of options were available,  
primarily geared toward upper middle-class residents. \(see  
also 1974, Lauderdale tower\)  
Housing in the Post-war Era  
1955–85  
Mikrorayon residential districts \(USSR\)  
50 million apartments were built in the Soviet Union  
between 1955 and 1985. Using quantitative design charts,  
sun diagrams, and a multitude of standardized building  
elements, innumerable iterations of the so-called “Mikroray-  
ons” were realized – prefabricated residential districts with  
clearly defined community facilities. The Mikrorayon system  
had an impact on almost all member states of the former  
Eastern Bloc and was later also influential in housing  
construction in the People’s Republic of China.  
Urbanization Processes in Latin America  
1955–57  
23 de Enero housing development, Caracas \(VE\) – Villa-  
nueva  
This residential district with a total of 9,108 apartments was  
regarded as an exemplary development scheme for the  
entire country. Elevators lead to external access galleries on  
every fourth floor, which connect to double-loaded  
staircases to the apartments on the other floors. At the front  
of the units, a living room and kitchen form day areas, while  
private rooms are arranged in a row at the rear.  
Urbanization Processes in Latin America  
1956-60  
Brasília \(BR\) – Lucio Costa \(master plan\)  
Brazil’s new capital was built in only four years and adhered  
in every respect to the CIAM principles of a functionally  
segregated city. Each “Superquadra” contains roughly 600  
apartments in nine to 11 slab buildings resting on pilotis  
above the open cityscape. Taken together, four such blocks  
and shared community facilities form a neighborhood zone.  
\(see also 1959, SQS 308\) A Chronicle of Developments in Housing | Oliver Heckmann, Eric Zapel  
Housing in the Post-war Era  
1957  
Interbau, Hansaviertel, Berlin \(GER\) – Otto Bartning  
\(overall design\), Kreuer Jobst \(master plan\), Otto Senn  
\(apartment building\)  
A model for the “city of tomorrow” – and a reaction in the  
West to the “Stalinallee” \(see also 1952–60\) – the ensemble  
of high-rises, slab and low-rise buildings was arranged in  
the middle of a landscaped urban park on a site where the  
urban block structure had been heavily damaged in the war.  
Renowned architects of the time like Le Corbusier, Aalto, van  
den Broek and Bakema, Gropius, Niemeyer or Senn designed  
and realized exemplary ideas on modern housing. In Senn’s  
apartment building, three individual apartment types fan  
out around a central and natural lit circulation area. Each  
apartment has views in two directions.  
Urbanization Processes in North Africa  
1957  
Climat de France, Algiers \(DZ\) – Fernand Pouillon  
An elongated superblock with an inner courtyard  
surrounded by a high colonnade forms the center of the  
social housing district, composed of diverse building types  
derived partly from regional housing typologies. Above the  
abundant shops on the ground floor, 360 deep apartments  
stretch between the street facade perforated with small  
windows and the interior colonnade, which provides  
protection from direct sunlight.  
New Building Type\: Large-scale Estates  
with Space-enclosing Structures  
Space-enclosing structures in taller and more  
meandering forms were implemented in  
the 1960s and 1970s to ensure the creation  
of protected inner spaces despite the high  
density of such projects on an urban scale –  
in contrast to the much-criticized open slab  
developments. Cityscapes accentuated by  
sporadic towers emerged, where spacious  
open areas free of traffic noise surrounded  
the buildings – large-scale sculptures for the  
society of the future. Many of these develop-  
ments implemented cluster architecture  
consisting of similar floor plan segments  
arranged in rows, stacked, or turned to  
optimize natural lighting or views. As time  
passed, these large-scale developments were  
criticized as mono-functional and inhospi-  
table bedroom communities, their build-  
ings characterized as “residential silos” and  
“concrete fortresses.”  
New Towns  
1957  
Gleadless Valley, Sheffield \(UK\) – J. L. Womersley  
This development was built on three sides of a wooded  
valley outside the city at the same time as Park Hill. Varied  
building designs with approximately 4,500 apartments in  
total are divided into three “neighborhoods” connected by  
walkways, each with its own shops and schools. Row houses  
are in part accessed from a protruding terrace on their  
upper level, three-story units connected to form  
cross-shaped buildings.  
Urbanization Processes in Latin America/Residential  
High-rises  
1957–66  
Edifício Copan, São Paulo \(BR\) – Oscar Niemeyer  
One of the tallest residential buildings of its time, this  
sinuous high-rise slab in the center of São Paulo was  
created with a diverse residential population in mind. The  
apartment types span from studio apartments occupying a  
single bay with sanitary rooms and kitchens in the darker,  
rear zone to 350 m² apartments with separate servants’  
quarters. The separate elevator core is connected with  
ramps and staircases to the main volume. Central corridors  
and access galleries behind the deep lamellae of the facade  
lead to the apartments entrances. In addition, a number of  
apartments are directly accessed from private elevators.  
Large-scale Estates with Space-enclosing Structures  
1957–61  
Park Hill, Sheffield \(UK\) – J. L. Womersley  
On a hill with a view over the city, the large-scale structure  
replaced an impoverished working-class district. The bent  
slabs follow the topography of the site. In the south,  
four-story building segments engage the surrounding lower  
buildings, while in the north, the building stands 13 stories  
tall. “Streets in the Air” on every third floor lead to  
apartment groups laid out across three bays and consisting  
of two studio apartments below and two maisonette units  
with open living areas above.  
Mass Housing in Asia  
1958  
Harumi Apartments, Tokyo \(JP\) – Kunio Maekawa  
Built by the Japanese Housing Association, this building  
remained exceptional in scale in Japan due to the  
traditionally small parcel structure. The high-rise slab  
combines a building structure inspired by Le Corbusier with  
traditional Japanese interior components, thus enabling a  
high degree of flexibility within the extremely compact  
apartments. \(see page 187\)  
1958
