27 Housing in the Post-war Era  
1958  
Extension of the “Ring” Estate, Berlin-Siemensstadt  
\(GER\) – Hans Scharoun  
So-called “residential homesteads” with community  
functions were formulated in conjunction with freely  
shaped slab buildings – as an extension and alternative to  
the more rigid structures from the 1930s. The “Symbiosis”  
apartment type combined a 4½ and a 1-room apartment  
with individual entrances, which could be combined or  
separated from one another. \(see also 1930, Siemensstadt\)  
Slab Housing  
1959  
Slab type SQS 308, Brasilia \(BR\) – Marcelo Campello and  
Sérgio Rocha  
Double-loaded staircases lead to apartments with separate  
service and servants’ quarters along one of the long facades.  
The dining area adjacent to the kitchen is the center. The  
living room is offset to one side, and the bedroom wing with  
its own corridor on the other. Brise-soleil or loggias shield  
the units from the heat of the sun. \(see also 1956, Brasília\)  
1958  
Mass Housing in Asia  
1958  
Hongshunli Commune, Tianjin \(CN\)  
Due to an extreme housing shortage, individual living space  
in the young People’s Republic of China was very limited,  
which – on top of dogma proclaiming the virtues of the  
commune over those of the individual – led to a search for  
compact housing arrangements. For this reason, all  
activities that could be organized collectively were practiced  
in the common areas. Residential units consequently  
consisted only of two bedrooms, the bathrooms were  
shared, communal canteens replaced individual kitchens,  
sewing rooms and nurseries were offered as additional  
amenities.  
Mass Housing in Asia  
Since 1960  
Building and Apartment Types, Singapore \(SG\) – Housing  
Development Board  
Between 1960 and 1965, the HDB built about 120,000 largely  
standardized apartment units in equally standardized  
housing blocks. Over the years, apartment standards, sizes,  
and variety increased and by 2000 more than 85 % of the  
population lived in HDB apartments. Subsidized home  
ownership and social mixing prevented the rise of many  
problems experienced in Western large-scale developments.  
28  
Low Rise High Density  
1962–1995  
Puchenau Estate, Linz \(AT\) – Roland Rainer  
The result of over three decades of building and research  
into three topics\: minimum parcel sizes, economic  
access systems, and passive solar thermal energy. From  
the four-story buildings with vertical access cores along  
the street to the one-story houses on the river, a formally  
and socially mixed estate design with a total of 984  
housing units emerges that follows the cadence of the  
landscape.  
Alternative Concepts  
During the 1960s, when the immediate  
housing crises of the post-war years had  
been alleviated and economies rebuilt,  
other aspects came to the foreground in  
Western societies. These were primar-  
ily incited by younger generations, who  
questioned the current practices and  
paradigms of society at large, but also  
within the architectural discourse of the  
time. The members of Team 10 countered  
the dogma of urban functional segregation  
with an alternative model that emerged  
from Candilis and Woods’ well-known eth-  
nographic studies of organically developed  
contexts in Casablanca, and which empha-  
sized the relationship between housing  
unit, building, street, district, and city.  
Projects like Archigram’s “Walking Cities,”  
Constant Nieuwenhuys’ “New Babylon”  
or Cedric Price’s “Fun Palace” developed  
visionary concepts that anticipated social  
mobility and cultural change.  
Low Rise High Density  
1963  
The Ryde, London \(UK\) – Phippen, Randall, Parkes  
A diverse patio housing estate with high living  
quality despite minimal unit widths and a simple  
construction method. 28 staggered, one-story  
houses are structured by shear walls protruding into  
the exterior space, which divide the houses and  
their tightly interlocking outdoor spaces from one  
another. With the exception of the kitchen and  
bathroom, almost all interior rooms are enclosed  
with sliding walls and subsequently use-neutral.  
\(see page 350\)  
Alternative Concepts  
1962  
Open Building \(NL\) – N. John Habraken, SAR  
Postulated as an “Alternative to Mass Housing,” the concept  
proposed dividing the built environment into two distinct  
categories\: a support system as the only part for which an  
architect would still be needed and an infill system placed  
in the hands of the residents so that housing could be  
adapted to individual needs. This approach was concerned  
with both the residential environment \(SAR 73\) as well as  
with the buildings themselves \(SAR 65\). A diagram from 1967  
illustrates the principle of the twin structures\: the support  
system is shown as parallel bands with predefined zones for  
the variable infill system comprised of common, service and  
circulation areas. A Chronicle of Developments in Housing | Oliver Heckmann, Eric Zapel  
Large-scale Estates with Space-enclosing Structures  
1963–1974  
Märkisches Viertel, Berlin \(GER\) – W. Düttmann,  
H. C. Müller, G. Heinrichs  
Space-enclosing, staggered, and linked buildings form large  
semi-courtyards; pre-existing detached single-family homes  
were left intact and complemented. This residential  
complex demonstrated a new trend toward increased  
vertical density in housing estates. \(see also 1969,  
Wilhelmsruher Damm and page 272\)  
New Building Type\: Terraced Buildings  
New ideas concerning terraced buildings as  
artificial landscapes emerged concurrent  
with the visions of Kenzo Tange, Walter  
Jonas, and Yona Friedman, who proposed  
city figures that hover above the ground  
and branch out horizontally and vertically.  
This new building type provided a chance  
to outfit every apartment with an open,  
outdoor space, while still generating a high  
level of built density. In cases where the  
terraced building did not follow an existing  
slope, the deep building interior on the  
lower floors was used for ancillary func-  
tions. Similar to other building types of the  
time, floor plan clusters were often imple-  
mented so that the overall expression was  
not defined by clear geometric volumes,  
but by a spatial structure that could seem-  
ingly continue on indefinitely.  
Large-scale Estates with Space-enclosing Structures  
1966  
Cité des Courtillières, Pantin \(FR\) – Émile Aillaud  
As an alternative to the typical formal rigidity of large-scale  
complexes to date, a six-story ribbon-like slab building  
surrounded by star-shaped high-rises encloses an inner,  
landscaped park. The simple and economic floor plan with  
two groups of rooms meant it was possible to later combine  
them into larger apartments.  
Terraced Buildings  
1963  
Dubiner Apartments, Tel Aviv \(IL\) – Neumann, Hecker,  
Sharon  
The intimacy of small-scale, vernacular forms of housing is  
combined with the efficiency of a terraced building on a sloping  
site. Stacked, hexagonal modules form the basic structure. A  
long corridor running along the terraces connects the bedrooms  
to the deep, polygonal living room and kitchen.  
New Towns  
1967–1970  
Thamesmead New Town, London \(UK\) – Robert Rigg \(GLC\)  
An ensemble of differentiated building types proposed as  
an alternative to the monotony of the early New Towns\:  
residential high-rises increase the overall density, higher  
slabs along the river shelter lower-lying row houses with  
stacked maisonettes and community buildings. Most  
buildings are connected through a complex system of  
pathways raised one story above the ground – a typical  
model in which pedestrians are separated from automobile  
traffic, but also a protection measure against flooding. In  
the maisonettes, the kitchen, hall, and staircases form a  
buffer to the public space.  
Terraced Buildings  
1967  
Habitat, Montreal \(CA\) – Moshe Safdie  
The housing units of this experimental project created for the  
EXPO 67 consisted of 55 m² modules in a total of 16 different  
constellations. The basic module contains the entrance, living  
room, and kitchen; additional modules are attached vertically  
or horizontally. The interlocking configurations form a  
complex terrace-like structure with intriguing outdoor spaces  
and pathways. \(see page 258\)  
1967  
29 1968  
Alternative Concepts  
1968–77  
Marquess Road, London \(UK\) – Darbourne and Darke  
As if along streets, apartments are strung along outdoor  
pathways on different levels; the rows are continually  
interrupted to create smaller groups of neighboring units.  
The apartments are primarily organized vertically\: two- to  
three-story back-to-back maisonettes are accessed through  
private front yards. In section, their upper levels cross over  
to the other side to benefit from views in multiple  
directions. \(see page 274\) Alternative Concepts/Low Rise High Density  
1968–75  
Proyecto Experimental de Vivienda \(PREVI\), Lima \(PE\)  
– Peter Land \(master plan\), Charles Correa/Candilis,  
Josic, Woods \(housing type designs\)  
Architects from around the world conceived the layout and  
model houses for originally 1,500 low-rise units as an  
alternative to informal developments on the urban  
periphery. Their aim was the development of basic,  
prefabricated units for cost-effective “growing houses” –  
simple yet intelligent spatial structures that could be  
expanded by their inhabitants. In the design by Charles  
Correa, units with alternating narrow and wide modules  
interlock. The wide modules can accommodate larger  
rooms or be divided in two separate spaces, and the narrow  
modules ancillary functions like sanitary rooms. Staircases  
can be incorporated into the “side pockets.” In the design by  
Candilis, Josic and Woods parallel rows of linked room  
sequences – with larger rooms or patios in the wide rows,  
smaller bedrooms or service spaces in the narrow rows –  
border each in such a way that different floor plan layouts  
are possible.  
30  
Low Rise High Density  
1968  
Oriental Masonic Gardens, New Haven \(USA\) – Paul  
Rudolph  
An estate as a spatial system for low-income residents.  
Prefabricated container-like elements are arranged like a  
windmill and stacked to form two-story units with living  
areas below and bedrooms on the level above, which is  
rotated 90°, generating space for a garden in the  
resulting corners.  
Residential High-rises  
1969  
Wilhelmsruher Damm 165–185, Märkisches Viertel, Berlin  
\(GER\) – O. M. Ungers  
Service tracts \(with entrance hall, kitchen, and WC\) and  
bedroom tracts \(with individual rooms, bathrooms, and a  
separate corridor\) surround the predominantly L-shaped living  
rooms of the apartments. These floor plan clusters shape the  
external appearance of the buildings\: connected groups of  
space-enclosing tower chains formed from stacked housing  
units. \(see also 1963–74, Märkisches Viertel and page 273\)  
Alternative Concepts  
1969–82  
Byker Regeneration, Newcastle upon Tyne \(UK\) – Ralph  
Erskine  
Instead of creating a master plan, a “plan of intent” formed  
the starting point for a mold-breaking participatory design  
process for both the public spaces and the apartment  
layouts. While 80 % of the housing units consist of low-rise  
row house types, the prominent Byker Wall wrapping around  
the northern edge of the estate was built to form a noise  
barrier to a planned thoroughfare. Very compact flats and  
maisonette apartments are accessed either from the ground  
floor or from external access galleries that double as social  
interfaces.  
Alternative Concepts  
1969–92  
Daikanyama Hillside Terrace, Tokyo \(JP\) – Fumihiko Maki  
This project was realized over multiple decades and makes  
use of Maki’s concept of the “Group Form” as a strategy for  
urban change\: a steadily, adaptively growing group of  
buildings along an eclectic series of public and semi-public  
spaces forge subtle transitions between the apartments and  
the city, between the private and the public. While the  
apartment floor plans of the first phase were simple and  
Modernist in nature, later types became more complex,  
adapting traditional Japanese principles for multilayered  
facades, making use of balconies and corridors as buffer  
zones, oriented toward calm courtyards.  
Alternative Concepts/Low Rise High Density  
1971–73  
Kasbah, Hengelo \(NL\) – Piet Blom  
Under a raised patchwork-like cluster of houses, the  
concept called for the creation of an open urban space  
below, which residents could use for commercial and social  
initiatives, even at times adapting the space to their needs.  
Each apartment type consists of the same two-story basic  
unit to which additional modules can be connected\: roof  
gardens, additional rooms above the paths and streets  
below or an entirely new unit. \(see page 302\) A Chronicle of Developments in Housing | Oliver Heckmann, Eric Zapel  
Asian New Towns  
While fewer and fewer large-scale estates  
were realized in Western societies, mass  
housing in the growing industrial countries  
of Asia remains the dominant housing  
form to this day – often with high-rises as  
a high density building type. In contrast  
to the West, these configurations are not  
primarily intended for low-income renters,  
but instead were built for the growing mid-  
dle class – predominantly with a model of  
home ownership in mind to help finance  
retirement. Cluster floor plans increasingly  
define the building shapes.  
Asian New Towns/Residential High-rises  
1971–81  
Sui Wo Court, Hong Kong \(HK\) – Palmer & Turner  
Subsidized home ownership in an ensemble consisting of  
nine high-rises in Sha Tin, one of the New Towns inspired by  
British models and built in the New Territories. The  
cross-shaped cluster floor plan is laid out around stacked  
three-story halls accessed by elevator on their lower level,  
which connects every 36 apartments into a neighborhood.  
The units are for the most part accessed from secondary  
staircases and open into the living room, from which a  
separate corridor leads to the individual rooms.  
Asian New Towns/Residential High-rises  
1970  
Seoseoul Apartments, Ahyeon-Dong/Sibeom  
Apartments, Yeouido \(SK\)  
Since the 1970s, residential high-rise estates have become  
the dominant form of housing in South Korea – realized by  
private developers instead of public entities and often  
replacing older, more organically evolved neighborhoods.  
By 2000, the number of residents in such developments  
rose to 50 % of the entire population. Thus, South Korea  
became the “Republic of Apartments.” In most of the  
similarly laid-out apartments, the living room takes up the  
motive of the central courtyard in traditional Korean Hanok  
housing types.  
1972  
Pruitt-Igoe, St. Louis \(USA\), beginning of  
demolition work  
For the architectural theorist Charles  
Jencks, the demolition of the failed large-  
scale housing development Pruitt-Igoe  
\(built 1951–56 – Minoru Yamasaki\) signified  
the “death of modern architecture.” But  
like many other such estates, its demise  
was not only the consequence of the  
anonymity of the architecture with its poor  
quality standards and lack of attention  
paid to communal issues. Originally de-  
signed for the middle class, its realization  
coincided with the exodus of better-off  
families to the newly built suburbs. These  
estates very quickly turned into ghettos,  
which in some cases eventually led to huge  
social problems accompanied by high  
vacancy rates.  
Residential High-rises  
1972  
Nagakin Capsule Tower, Tokyo \(JP\) – Kisho Kurokawa  
Industrially prefabricated capsules were meant to be easily  
installable on the load-bearing cores, to be exchanged at  
any point or to be flexibly linked to one another. The  
compact units are only the size of four tatami mats, have  
integrated built-in furniture and bathing cells and were  
conceptualized as work-live cells.  
Residential High-rises  
1974  
Lauderdale Tower, Barbican Estate, London \(UK\) –  
Chamberlin, Powell, Bon  
The three single-orientation, elongated 5-room apartments  
with corner living rooms opening up to loggias and escape  
balconies are functionally clearly structured and generate a  
cluster-like, triangular high-rise. \(see also 1955–83,  
Barbican\)  
1973  
Gallaratese II, Milan \(IT\) – Aldo Rossi  
Up to this point, Rossi was only known as a theorist who  
spurred on important debates about the architectural  
relevance of the historic city. The elongated slab structures  
were part of a collaborative design for a new housing  
development. Clearly structured, two-tier apartment plans  
reveal a kitchen and bathroom placed along an external  
access gallery and a connecting hallway leading to all other  
spaces of the two-room apartments.  
Residential High-rises  
1974  
Walden 7, Barcelona \(ES\) – Ricardo Bofill, Taller de  
Arquitectura  
Tower segments laid out in a checkerboard grid and  
connected around courtyards to form a complex high-rise  
cluster. A network of circulation paths on every second floor  
unites the individual apartments composed of 30 m²  
modules into a community. Instead of walls and hallways,  
only built-in furniture and varying floor levels zone the  
flowing spaces of the units. \(see page 240\)  
31  
1974 1974  
Residential High-rises/Terraced Buildings  
1976–85  
Alt-Erlaa Residential Park, Vienna \(AT\) – Harry Glück  
Described as a “vertical garden city,” this large-scale housing  
scheme with terraced high-rises, onto which the green  
surroundings seemingly continue to grow, are remarkably  
well-liked despite the interior corridors having no windows  
and the apartments being oriented to one side only. The  
numerous community spaces in the belly of the building  
nurture neighborly contact, while spacious terraces provide  
a replacement for one’s own garden.  
Residential High-rises  
1976  
Pearl Bank Apartments, Singapore \(SG\) – Tan Cheng  
Siong, Archurban  
At 38 stories, the tallest residential high-rise in the city-state  
at the time and a response to a prolonged period of growth.  
The circulation system is located on the inner facade of the  
open cylinder and composed of external access galleries  
and branching secondary staircases. The varied apartment  
types are organized as split-levels, with open, dual-oriented  
living areas spanning between the inner circle and the  
exterior facade.  
Postmodernism/Back to the City  
With the increasing influence of  
Postmodernism and, particularly in Europe,  
the rediscovery of the city as an attrac-  
tive place to live, small-scale and diverse  
solutions began to appear as did projects  
searching for urban and architectural  
historic continuities. Partially influenced  
by discussions around the European  
Architectural Heritage Year 1975 as well  
as growing criticism of post-war planning  
and the resulting deserted city centers,  
the long neglected historic districts were  
gaining more appreciation. Block infill  
projects and the modernization of existing  
buildings were becoming an option and  
apartment floor plans less schematic, as  
they had to take local circumstances into  
consideration.  
Back to the City  
1976  
Calle Doña Maria Coronel, Seville \(ES\) – Cruz, Ortiz  
A kidney-shaped courtyard becomes a sculptural void  
on this irregular building site in the historic city center.  
Each apartment adapts very differently to the complex  
geometry, turning toward additional patios through  
crooked corridors, leading hallways around the  
courtyard or making use of the living room as an  
entrance and distribution center. The roof terraces and  
the courtyard are shared spaces. \(see page 104\)  
Low Rise High Density  
1974  
Galgebakken, Copenhagen \(DK\) – Storgård,  
Orum-Nielsen, Marcussen  
A car-free estate providing a common site for diverse  
housing needs. Small groups of atrium houses, row houses  
and split-level housing types are bundled together with  
entrances, eat-in kitchens and at times play areas  
overlooking shared pathways or courtyards. For the most  
part, dually oriented living rooms open up to a garden at the  
rear. \(see page 306\)  
Second Demographic Shift\:  
Diversification  
Around the beginning of the 1970s, the so-  
called “second demographic shift,” which  
covers both increasingly diverse familial  
relations – later and lower birth rates,  
higher divorce rates, and an increasing  
number of unmarried couples – as well as  
changing social values – like the increasing  
relevance of self-actualization and equal  
gender roles – also had an impact on  
housing concepts. Besides the classic nu-  
clear family, diverse forms of cohabitation  
were developing, which were also often no  
longer conceived of as long-term solutions.  
Individual demands were formulated,  
which could no longer be satisfied by the  
dominant standard solutions of the time.  
Participation and self-building options  
changed approaches to design floor plan  
types were becoming more diverse and  
flexible.  
Alternative Concepts/Diversification  
1976  
Diagoon Houses, Delft \(NL\) – Herman Hertzberger  
The housing units were intentionally left incomplete\:  
staircases, technical installations and the split-level-like  
diagonally offset floors were predetermined. The  
functional use and decision to enclose or leave the  
rooms open was left up to the discretion of the  
residents. \(see page 352\)  
Low Rise High Density  
1977  
Quinta da Malagueira, Évora \(PO\) – Álvaro Siza  
Affordable housing with a total of 1,200 units arranged  
in a back-to-back configuration of L-shaped row houses  
with private patios. Residents are given a footprint of 8 x  
12 m. The basic unit houses a kitchen, bathroom and  
living and bedrooms on the ground floor with the  
possibility to expand the surface area with a maximum  
of four additional bedrooms on the second floor.  
32 A Chronicle of Developments in Housing | Oliver Heckmann, Eric Zapel  
Back to the City  
1979  
Herter House, Munich \(GER\) – Hilmer & Sattler  
A typical inner-city floor plan solution for the time, with a  
large hall as the spatial focal point.  
Alternative Concepts/Diversification  
1977  
Molenvliet, Papendrecht \(NL\) – Van der Werf, Werkgroep  
KOKON  
This project tested the “Open Building” principle of support  
and infill systems as well as participatory design methods  
for both the residential environment and building  
structures. Grouped around four car-free courtyards – thus  
enabling the creation of small, intimate neighborhoods  
– the overall structure, on a 4.80 m grid which remained  
open in two directions, was first parceled according to  
individual space requirements and then built out in close  
consultation with the wishes of the residents. Its capability  
to be freely subdivided made it possible to create a variety  
of housing types from studio apartments to 6-room  
arrangements and the accommodation of both housing and  
commercial units. \(see also 1962, Open Building\)  
Back to the City/ Building Exhibitions and Model Estates  
1984–87  
International Building Exhibition Berlin \(GER\)  
Two conceptual topics stand for a new, exemplary approach  
to urban redevelopment. In contrast to the previous  
dominance of the automotive city and large-scale  
demolitions, which sacrificed many older, organically  
evolved urban structures, “critical reconstruction” sought to  
reconstruct and increase density in inner-city areas with the  
historic city layout as its basis. Cautious urban renewal – an  
approach which grew out of the protest milieu of the  
squatting movement – pursued the preservation and  
rehabilitation of older housing stock. It also intended to  
stabilize and further develop existing social and functional  
structures within the urban districts – using the instruments  
of participation, self-help and modernization measures  
carried out by the inhabitants themselves.  
Back to the City/Building Exhibitions and Model Estates  
1985  
Rauchstraße, Berlin \(GER\) – Rob Krier \(master plan\), Hans  
Hollein \(urban villa\)  
One of the most widely discussed urban compositions of the  
IBA was the solitary building ensemble on Rauchstraße, which  
transferred the suburban building type of the “Urban Villa” to  
the city center and adapted it for use as social housing. 10  
solitary buildings with a footprint of 21 x 21 m each were  
designed by different architects, and together with the end  
building by Rob Krier, surround a large garden courtyard. In the  
diagonally laid- out apartments, an octagon-shaped hall,  
which connects to almost every other room, leads directly  
from the entrance into the corner living room.  
Back to the City  
1986  
Schrankenberggasse, Vienna \(AT\) – Rob Krier  
This building with clear references to historic architectural  
elements combines a corner building with an infill typology.  
Symmetrically arranged living rooms in the axes of the  
entrances serve – particularly in the dually oriented corner  
apartments – as representative circulation centers. \(see  
page 107\)  
Alternative Concepts  
1979  
Odham’s Walk, London \(UK\) – Donald Ball  
Residents’ protests in Covent Garden led to the design of a  
lively residential quarter instead of a planned wholesale  
redevelopment. While ateliers, workshops, and exhibition  
spaces on the ground floor anchor the complex to the  
surrounding streets, 2- to 6-room apartments above  
encircle multiple interlocking courtyards. The maisonette  
apartments at the top are accessed from an external gallery  
that winds its way around the courtyards.  
Residential High-rises  
1983  
Kanchanjunga Apartments, Mumbai \(IN\) – Charles Correa  
A high-rise for wealthy residents is optimally laid out for the  
climate\: both the building and the open, split-level floor  
plans are oriented to maximize cross ventilation from the  
dominant wind patterns. An outdoor layer of loggias – in the  
form of deeply drawn-in, double-height verandas at the  
corners of the building – help shield against direct sunlight  
and monsoon rains. \(see page 242\)  
1986  
33 Urban Revitalization  
Extensive, centrally located areas whose  
original purpose as industrial or transporta-  
tion sites has become obsolete as a result  
of structural changes and technological  
innovation, were used for urban revitaliza-  
tion strategies. Projects such as IJplein,  
KNSM- & Java-eiland or Borneo Sporenburg in  
Amsterdam, HafenCity in Hamburg, Nyhavn in  
Copenhagen, Docklands in Melbourne or the  
port area of Toronto offered a unique oppor-  
tunity to set a large-scale accent – not only  
for the reanimation of areas near the inner  
city, but also for new urban housing models.  
Back to the City  
1991  
Rue de Meaux, Paris \(FR\) – Renzo Piano  
The complex extends across an entire block and is  
simultaneously infl uenced by both the surrounding block  
edge and courtyard building types to form a consistent  
urban ensemble. The fl oor plans are clearly structured\: a  
dually oriented, divisible living room is located in the  
mid-section of the single-story fl ats, with the kitchen and  
access area on the one side, bedrooms with a secondary  
corridor and bathrooms on the other.  
Urban Revitalization  
1988  
IJplein, Amsterdam \(NL\) – OMA \(master plan, building  
design\)  
The original high-rise ensemble planned for a new  
urban district in a former shipyard directly across from  
the city center was changed to rows of parallel blocks  
due to protests. The fl oor plans are dominated by stairs  
– transversely connecting single-story fl ats at different  
junctures, and lengthwise within the maisonettes.  
1987  
1987  
Avenue du Général Leclerc, Nîmes \(FR\) – Nouvel, Ibos  
Wide wraparound galleries on every second fl oor provide  
access to the maisonettes and single-story apartments  
on the north side and function as balconies to the south.  
By setting the shear walls at 5 m intervals, the individual  
units can be reconfi gured without any structural  
constraints, making it possible to create very diverse  
apartment types. In order to increase the amount of living  
space for each unit, standards were dramatically reduced,  
industrial building components used and walls left  
unfi nished. \(see page 194\)  
Low Rise High Density  
1988–93  
CIDCO Housing, Navi Mumbai \(IN\) – Raj Rewal  
Part of a larger urban development scheme, the project is  
considered a model for affordable housing in a low-rise,  
pedestrian-friendly and dense building structure. A range of  
housing units brings together not only different sized  
families but also diverse income groups. Cubic volumes in a  
cluster-like composition form ensembles up to three stories  
in height around common courtyards with roof terraces at  
various levels.  
Housing at the End of the 20th Century  
Housing at the end of the 20th century is  
exceptionally heterogeneous. Neoliberal  
patterns of thought influenced hous-  
ing policies and in the 1990s led to the  
withdrawal of public funding from social  
housing in a number of Western societies.  
With this development, many restrictive  
measures were loosened and alterna-  
tive approaches filled the resulting gaps.  
However, this also led to an increasing  
shortage of affordable housing. With the  
demise of Postmodernism – which was  
increasingly criticized as self-referential  
and purely formal – architects again  
increasingly turned towards more concrete  
problems and developments. They began  
to design more conceptually, and eco-  
nomic and social aspects were considered  
anew. Simultaneously, residential buildings  
outside of Europe and North America  
increasingly influenced the debate.  
Building Exhibitions and Model Estates/  
Low Rise High Density  
1991  
Nexus, Fukuoka \(JP\) – OMA  
Three-story, compact low rise high density structure  
consisting of two blocks of L-shaped residential units  
with private patios accessed from a network of pathways on  
the ground fl oor. A roof terrace serves as a complementary  
outdoor space; wave-like shed roofs bring in additional  
natural lighting. \(see page 308, see also 1962, Open Building\)  
34 A Chronicle of Developments in Housing | Oliver Heckmann, Eric Zapel  
Urban Revitalization  
1992  
Borneo Sporenburg, Amsterdam \(NL\) – West 8 \(master  
plan\), Höhne & Rapp \(row house\)  
The area was to be redeveloped to create an attractive, urban  
neighborhood for families near the city center. A high level of  
diversity was achieved through a mix of housing tenure  
models – 30 % public and 70 % privately financed residential  
units – and various building types – predominantly low rise  
high density structures combined with three larger multi-story  
apartment buildings. The owners of the 60 individual homes  
were allowed to choose their desired architect. \(see page 121\)  
Urban Revitalization  
1994  
Piraeus, Amsterdam \(NL\) – Jo Coenen \(master plan\),  
Hans Kollhoff, with Christian Rapp \(building\)  
Based on a design with conventional urban blocks, the  
building type was deformed to preserve an existing historic  
building on the site. The resulting complex figure brought  
about the development of a multifaceted access system and  
numerous apartment types. A consistent motive in the  
apartments is an inserted core with a kitchen and living  
room to one side, and a corridor to the other. \(see page 86\)

This superblock is located in a harbor basin between the  
city center and Borneo Sporenburg and offers a multitude of  
apartment types, whose heterogeneity is reflected in the  
complex access system and diverse facade treatments.

Alternative Concepts  
1994  
Next 21, Osaka \(JP\) – Utida et al.  
A test model for sustainable housing designed according to  
the theories of John Habraken\: an open frame structure  
similar to a shelving unit can be occupied with  
individualized and exchangeable housing units. \(see also  
1962, Open Building and page 156\)  
1998  
Space Block Kamishinjo, Osaka \(JP\) – Kojima + Akamatsu  
To improve the lighting conditions on the narrow infill site, the  
porosity of the building mass was modelled. The foundation of  
the design is a system of modular spatial blocks that are  
stacked, spring back and forth and create recesses to increase  
the surface area of the building’s envelope, thus admitting  
more daylight. In this manner, spatially complex  
mini-apartments are generated in the interior. \(see page 118\)  
2000  
Kitagata Residential Building \(JP\) – Kazuyo Sejima,  
Nishizawa  
Rather than predefining housing units, various types of  
spaces \(private room, tatami room, in part  
double-height kitchens and loggias oriented to two  
sides\) can be combined and accessed lalong both  
facades from both a public and private walkway, making  
various cohabitation models possible. \(see page 208\)  
Alternative Concepts  
1998–2000  
Miss Sargfabrik, Vienna \(AT\) – BKK3  
As a complement to the previously built project “Sargfabrik”  
– a converted factory site – a cooperative initiated, designed  
with a participatory approach and realized a building that  
sought to combine collective ways of living, communal  
facilities and work-life scenarios.  
1994  
Bahnhofstrasse, Graz \(AT\) – Riegler Riewe Architekten  
An exemplary case of polyvalent housing, which can  
accommodate very diverse occupancies due to its versatile  
transverse and lengthwise circulation possibilities, the multi-part  
service zone in the middle and almost identically sized rooms.

35 Oliver Heckmann  
# Challenges and Tendencies  
In contrast to a review of the past – where the distance  
of time allows for a more accurate reflection on which  
historical, societal, and economic developments or design  
strategies challenged existing paradigms and contributed  
significantly to the development of housing – the present  
can only be described in tendencies. What is discussed here  
are some of the most important challenges facing urban  
housing today. Concrete examples are offered as illustrations  
of potential design solutions.  
The Social Segregation of Cities  
An increasing section of the population is threatened with  
exclusion from the real-estate market – whether as renters or  
buyers – because they simply cannot afford the rapidly acceler-  
ating cost of housing. This is not only due to the global financial  
crisis of 2007, but also to the increasing attractiveness of real  
estate as an investment, the often-cited gap in income develop-  
ment, and even factors like online vacation rental platforms that  
increasingly deprive cities attractive to tourists of sufficient  
residential space. The current scarcity of affordable housing  
is also further aggravated in Western industrial societies  
by the steady growth in the attractiveness of city life – with  
simultaneous high and still increasing numbers of empty  
properties in many rural regions. The situation is made more  
acute by processes of gentrification, where in well-located but  
1  
Dattner Architects, Grimshaw Architects\: Bronx Social Housing, New York, 2012  
The project, which was built as a model for “affordable, sustainable and  
reproducible” housing in a central location, combines three building types taken  
from the surroundings – a row of townhouses, a higher block with duplex units  
and a high-rise – to form a continuous building, the roof of which winds its way  
up from the courtyard to the uppermost roof terrace and can be used as a  
collective fruit and vegetable garden as well as for recreational activities. Small  
shops, a health center, work-life units and a fitness center enliven the juncture  
between the street and the courtyard.  
36  
often neglected neighborhoods, socially and ethnically diverse  
residents are pushed out by groups with higher incomes.  
This development requires counter measures, that with  
more inclusive concepts attempt to stem the trend toward  
social segregation within cities – which on a societal level  
can also be viewed critically – by creating new models for af-  
fordable housing, whether in the form of new building types,  
new property development models, through the renewal and  
expansion of social housing programs, cautious forms of in-  
creasing inner-city density as well as renewed, contemporary  
research into “housing for the minimum subsistence level.”  
The inhabitants to be considered are extremely heterogene-  
ous and span from singles, couples, families, retirees, and  
immigrants to groups on the edge of society such as the  
homeless 1, 2, 3.  
2  
Michael Maltzan Architects\: Star Apartments, Los Angeles, 2014  
In this residential project for homeless people, the goal to foster a  
social interface with the immediate surroundings through a mix of  
uses was applied to the redevelopment of an existing parking  
structure. The ground floor is occupied by a health center for  
residents and neighbors, and the level above acts as a podium with  
communal gardens, jogging path and meeting places. The  
prefabricated residential units stacked above each have their own  
bathroom and kitchen and are connected in an open spatial fabric.  
Open voids offering views of both the city beyond and the podium  
below, and numerous staircases and external galleries are meant  
to foster contact. Challenges and Tendencies | Oliver Heckmann  
8  
4 5  
HAHOH Haas Heckmann Architekten\: Am Ottersgraben House, Zell am  
Harmersbach, 2007  
A house with initially four compact apartments was to be built in such a way to  
keep open the option of completely different layouts. The scenarios included  
apartments for singles or couples, units for families as well as a multi-genera-  
tional house comprising all fl oors. The two staircases on the ground fl oor serve as  
intersection for various connections\: all adjacent areas – the units above and  
below, to the left and right as well as the stair landings – can be linked in a  
fl exible manner.  
Archplan AG\: Wohnfabrik Solinsieme, St. Gallen, 2002  
Solinsieme – a combination of the words “solo” \(alone\) and “insieme” \(together\)  
– is a participatory project initiated by the residents, all in the second half of their  
lives, and combines individual housing with a more community-based lifestyle.  
An existing factory was converted and expanded with a new building, which acts  
as a collective intersection comprising access areas and sun decks. In total, the  
shared spaces make up almost 20 % of the building surface and also include  
studios, guest rooms, a roof terrace, a bike store and a community room with  
enough space for events.  
DEMOGRAPHIC CHANGE\: GENERATIONS  
While the increasing quantitative imbalance between gen-  
erations is above all a social and economic problem, specifi c  
alternative housing strategies are also required for the differ-  
ent generations – with foresight into the different residential  
forms and locations inhabitants prefer during each phase of  
life or the fact that future generations may simply be much  
smaller in number.  
among many others. Familial relations and constellations  
are changing much quicker and more often than in previous  
times, thus increasing the demands on the fl exibility of both  
the individual apartment fl oor plan, as well as the way in  
which adjacent units can be connected to or separated from  
one another 4.  
earlier generations – for instance, a preference for collective  
or assisted living 5, 6.  
Families  
In particular the family – with the nuclear family as the  
main object of housing standardization research for a long  
time – has become more diversifi ed over the years. Besides  
the traditional model, there exist single-parent households,  
patchwork families, and multi-generational living situations,  
Baby Boomers  
As the baby boomer generation ages, the number of retirees  
is going to dramatically increase in the coming years. A grow-  
ing elderly population in many societies will have to be coun-  
tered with a considerable expansion of appropriate fl oor  
plan typologies and forms of living that are not only suitable  
for this demographic development, but also take into ac-  
count that seniors today increasingly prefer to live in urban  
areas or have other, more diverse wishes and demands than  
Millennials  
Among so-called millennials, who today range from 18 to 30  
years old, a number of signifi cant changes have also taken  
place\: increasingly infl uential to where and how one lives  
are not only longer periods of education, a desire to fi rst  
establish a career or the fact that marriage is more seldom,  
occurs later or not at all, while divorce rates increase, but  
also that in comparison to older generations they often earn  
considerably less and have fewer assets. While the more af-  
fl uent segment often lives alone in 2- to-3-room apartments,  
refl ective of the more general trend toward more living space  
per person, many millennials are increasingly excluded from  
the real-estate market. Breaking free of conventions and  
3  
5468796 Architecture + Cohlmeyer Architects\: Centre Village,  
Winnipeg, 2010  
In this project realized for underprivileged families, multi-story  
residential units – each with its own entrance – surround a  
communal courtyard and space for everyday interaction. The  
units are comprised of very compact, narrow room modules  
connected to form vertical and horizontal spatial sequences,  
which generate apartment types for the various needs of the  
diverse inhabitants. The organization of both the building and  
the individual units aims to reduce ineffi cient and unattractive  
access spaces in order to reduce rental costs as much as  
possible.  
6  
Javier García-Solera\: Assisted living with day-care facilities, Santa Vicente del  
Raspeig, Alicante, 2005  
Set in a new public parc, the project couples autonomous living for seniors with  
services and care above a day-care facility. The inserted bathrooms on both sides  
of the dividing walls cleverly zone the fl oor plans of the individual units\: on one  
side they subtly narrow the open transition from the living room to the bedroom,  
and on the other they are inserted fl ush with the built-in furniture of the kitchen  
and storage spaces.  
37 7  
Naruse Inokuma Architects\: LT Josai, Nagoya, 2013  
LT Josai reacts to a growing social trend in Japan in  
which singles decide to share a living space temporarily  
or more permanently without knowing one another  
beforehand. Their private rooms surround an open,  
split-level common space spanning all floors. Even the  
bathrooms are shared.  
8  
SsD Architecture\: Songpa Micro Housing, Seoul, 2014  
The spatial limitations of the micro apartments here are  
compensated for by semi-private open interstitial  
spaces that form shared thresholds through which  
multiple units can be combined. This matrix is  
complemented by “shared living rooms” that can be  
used by both residents and neighbors – a micro  
auditorium and a café in the lower level as well as a  
gallery and work spaces above the open ground floor.  
9  
narchitects\: Carmel Place, New York, 2016  
The project grew out of a publicly funded initiative to  
create affordable housing for the growing number of  
single-person households in New York. 22 of the  
20–33.5 m² apartments are subsidized, 33 are offered at  
market price. The modular, prefabricated micro  
apartments are centrally located in Manhattan and  
complemented by service and other shared facilities.  
increased flexibility has been helpful here\: downsizing has  
become an acceptable option, housing has become more  
temporary or is fundamentally not thought about in perma-  
nent terms, establishing a household and a family is often  
postponed. The number of single-person residences is again  
on the rise and shared accommodation is – beyond one’s  
time as a student – increasingly more attractive 7.  
Micro Apartments  
The development of micro apartments, based on the concept  
of serviced apartments from the hotel sector, is also an  
attempt to create living space in otherwise often unafford-  
able central urban areas. By implementing spatial scenarios  
that change throughout the day and built-in, multifunctional  
furniture like flexible tables, folding beds or compressible  
wardrobes – and at times a conscious removal of ancillary  
functions from the apartment or an increase in available  
resident services – they try to systematically minimize the  
required space for each individual. Their diverse manifesta-  
tions are inhabited by singles, business travelers or com-  
muters\: common among all iterations, however, is that living  
here often remains impersonal and asocial, as residents can  
bring in little personal furniture and it is difficult to entertain  
guests 8, 9.  
Outsourcing  
While such developments are indicative of a new trend in  
many metropolitan areas, they are a long-established stand-  
ard in the cities of Japan. The resulting building configura-  
tions are closely connected to the characteristics of their  
urban context, since original functions of living such as food  
preparation and eating have been in part “outsourced” to the  
public sphere. The forms of the urban and individual spheres  
complement and necessitate one another 10.  
DEMOGRAPHIC CHANGE\: DIVERSIFICATION  
The increasingly heterogeneous nature of society has led to  
a broad multiplicity of ideas on possible housing forms, a  
multiplicity that still cannot be satisfied by the existing hous-  
ing inventory 11, 12. But having adequate design ideas for  
these other modes of living is not only what matters, rather  
it must be accepted that the permanence of a chosen way  
of life – heretofore simply assumed as a constant – is hardly  
valid anymore.  
The diversification of apartment size, layout and stand-  
ards is an important criterion among all these trends when  
taking a variety of lifestyles and incomes into account. In or-  
-  
12  
Riken Yamamoto & Field Shop\: Local Community Area \(theoretical proposal\), 2012  
The “Local Community Area” concept questions the sustainability of the nuclear  
family as the smallest social entity and instead proposes an urban community with  
roughly 500 residents. The relationships between the private and collective spheres  
are completely rethought\: the family is no longer the only basis for shared economic  
activity or mutual and reciprocal care, and the exclusive rights to individual rooms  
are reduced to a minimum in favor of shared areas wherever possible. Two types of  
spaces are offered – for rent only, to ensure that constant adaptation to social  
changes remains a possibility. Open, transparent rooms can be used as commercial  
spaces, offices, studios, or as private verandas, forming an interface with the commu-  
nity. Enclosed units can be occupied as more conventional private households.  
Bathrooms, however, are shared, which is more efficient than providing each unit  
with its own.

On the entrance levels of the maisonette units,  
working spaces are lined up along the transparent  
central corridor. In the living spaces above and  
below, internal staircases, kitchenettes, bathrooms,  
and sliding doors serve as filters behind the  
floor-to-ceiling glazed facades.

Open living room typology \(4th fl oor\)  
der to potentially expand the choice, a broad range of differ-

standards – can be offered within one and the same project.  
Simultaneously, apartments with several access points allow  
for divisions or multiple uses 13, 14, 15.  
The Dominance of the Generic  
Many apartment fl oor plans still often functionally adhere to  
structures developed with the needs of the classic nuclear  
family in mind 16. In particular, large residential complexes  
realized by property developers show little interest in the  
creation of more contemporary fl oor plans – which may  
be due to potential buyers who purchase an apartment  
more as an investment preferring something non-specifi c,  
or that traditional patterns in mass housing are simply  
not questioned. Meanwhile, post-war housing estates are  
increasingly called into question due to the lack of fl exibility  
in their fl oor plans, while the highly esteemed, spacious  
and less defi ned fl oor plans of mid- to late-19th century  
European residential buildings seem to offer more fl exibility  
for changing living arrangements.  
Deprogramming  
Admittedly, too much diversifi cation can also be viewed  
critically, at least when it results in extreme spatial and  
functional determination, which can only be adapted with  
considerable efforts – in contrast to offi ce buildings, for  
example, where neutral fl oors are offered to accommodate  
the individual demands of each user. A small number of  
projects suggest a different path. Use-neutral buildings  
generate structures that incorporate the unpredictability  
of how the property will be utilized long-term, anticipating  
and allowing for uses besides housing, which are to a great  
degree vaguely defi ned and fl exible and may therefore easily  
be reprogrammed 17.  
Alternative models  
It is often up to smaller projects to show new directions that  
not only follow different design strategies, but above all else  
endeavor to establish alternative models of cohabitation,  
connections between working and living, or even property  
development 18, 19.  
Co-Housing  
Co-housing or cooperative building ventures take advantage  
of niches resulting from diversifi cation in the real-estate  
market, and react to the previously mentioned economic  
developments, which would otherwise preclude the par-  
16  
Generic fl oor plan type for the nuclear family  
This infl exible fl oor plan is optimized for the nuclear family,  
and in similar fashion realized in many condominium projects  
in Asian cities. The unit is accessed through the living room,  
from which a hallway leads to extremely compact bedrooms  
on one side, a kitchen and bathroom on the other, and at its  
end a master bedroom with its own bathroom.

Brandlhuber + NiehüserS Architekten\: 4-Direction Module,  
Hamburg 2013  
Because the ideal orientation for housing in northern Europe  
is east-west, while a north-south orientation is preferred for  
work, this proposal calls for a spatial cross of two correspond -  
ingly oriented room modules.

ANA\: Multifunk, Amsterdam-Ijburg, 2007  
The multifunctional building “Multifunk” is fl exible both in its  
individual units and in its entirety – apartments can be

of fl oor plans are possible\: standard fl oor plans, duplexes,

twin elevator shafts, and generous ceiling heights are further  
prerequisites for this fl exibility.

Urbanus\: Tulou Residential Commune,  
Nanhai, Guandong, 2008

ticipants from finding affordable and adequate housing in  
their preferred locations. In co-housing design, users are  
no longer mere consumers of predefined forms of housing  
but become active participants in the creation of the floor  
plan – both in the act of inhabiting but also in the develop-  
ment of the buildings. Ideas on collective living are thus  
formulated. In these typically rather small collaborative  
housing projects, the inhabitants, while often assuming  
the economic risks for the project, have the opportunity to  
adapt the standard and size of their units to their income  
during the planning and the building stages. They are able  
to realize their individual concepts of living and to form a  
community, even prior to the completion of the building.  
Flexible floor plan structures with intelligently placed load-  
bearing, access and technical structures allow for individual  
floor plan solutions 20, 21.  
FURTHER DEVELOPMENTS  
Global Urbanization  
According to a prognosis by the United Nations, in 2050,  
two-thirds of humanity will be living in cities worldwide. A  
significant portion of the global population growth will take  
place in urban areas, in particular in the so-called emerg-  
ing economies of Africa, Asia and Latin America. Migration  
patterns from rural regions to urban areas are primarily  
motivated by economic factors and present challenges to the  
demographic, economic, and social structures of both cities  
and rural areas 22, 23.  
Housing in growing and increasingly more permanent  
informal settlements and similar ways of living require strate-  
gies which – in lieu of demolition and forced resettlement  
of entire neighborhoods into places without any naturally  
evolved structures – build on legalization and participation,  
the stabilization of existing social networks, the creation of  
the necessary infrastructure to provide water supplies, sewers  
and drains, garbage removal, and more; strategies which  
employ simple means to design adequate and better quality  
housing forms with improved living conditions and economic  
possibilities 24.  
Emergency Housing  
The experience of recent years has shown how important  
it is to be prepared with adequate housing strategies for  
the enormous challenges posed by the immigration of  
refugees – whether their forced migration be due to natural  
catastrophes, violent conflict, economic reasons, or the  
consequences of climate change. Affordable, easily produced  
housing has to be created, but there is simultaneously a  
23 John Lin, Rural Urban Framework\: Shijia House, Shijia, 2012  
Against the backdrop of the massive population migrations to  
large Chinese cities, this house typology was developed as an  
alternative model to lend new vibrancy, sustainability and  
comfort to life in the countryside. By making use of natural  
resources, it sets an example for supply autonomy. The local  
artisanal production that takes place here contributes both to  
the village community and the local economy.  
21  
Case Architects\: TEN Bangkok, Bangkok, 2009  
Spurred on by a collectively organized loan, the building lot  
was divided into ten segments, which although individually  
designed, were developed in close collaboration with the  
neighbors. Inspired by a traditional Thai building type, the  
units enclose a common courtyard; kitchens remain open to  
the surroundings to provide better ventilation when cooking,  
facilities like the pool and laundry room are shared.  
-  
24  
Elemental\: Quinta Monroy, Iquique, Chile, 2004  
This project for affordable housing was devised to preserve,  
legalize, and stabilize the vital economic and social networks  
of an informal settlement. The empty voids between the units  
not only accommodate self-built additions needed for  
changing demands, but also increase the value of the house in  
order to, for example, secure a loan to open a business or  
finance a child’s education.

Kunihiro Ando + Satoyama Architecture Laboratory\:  
Itakura Emergency Housing, Iwaki, 2011  
In 2011, timber-framed houses were erected according to tradi-  
tional building methods to house the victims of the Tsunami  
in Japan. The decision to use wood was above all made to  
bolster the local economy. After their completion, a local  
woodworker taught the residents how to build furniture from  
the leftover material, which was meant to both aid in the  
personalization of the individual houses as well as to  
strengthen bonds within the community. The housing units  
are organized on two levels under an exposed wooden roof  
construction. The upper level can be used for sleeping, living,  
or as storage.

Feldschnieders + Kister, Temporary Housing for Asylum  
Seekers, Bremen, 2015  
12 units with a maximum of 16 people per floor in 2- to 4-person

from public to semi-public and private spheres. Four such  
courtyards enclose an open area with communal facilities.  
need for more long-term solutions whose structures not only  
anticipate their disassembly or conversion, but also whose  
placement and layout avoid the formation of ghettos and  
encourage social integration 25, 26.  
The complexity of all these trends cannot be tackled alone  
with static solutions. A design approach that aims to con-  
stantly identify questions, opportunities, and challenges in a  
spirit of curiosity is far more contemporary and sustainable.  
To this end, this book serves as a resource for reviewing floor  
plan solutions that have already – as an adequate answer to  
specific challenges – been invented, built, and inhabited.  
Asian Megacities  
Given their enormous growth, Asian megacities can serve  
as a field for experimentation in path-breaking solutions  
on a large scale, to generate sustainable, alternative ways of  
building for the economic, ecological, and social challenges  
of increasing urban density. This applies both to historic  
quarters, which until now – despite being part of the local  
urban identity – were often demolished to make way for new  
buildings, as well as to the construction of New Towns, which  
have to establish new urban neighborhoods without benefit-  
ting from existing social networks 27, 28.  
27  
Riken Yamamoto & Field Shop, Jian Wai SOHO, Beijing, 2004  
The multifunctional high-rise complex connects a field of towers, with home  
office units at their base, with an urban, multi-layered mesh of public  
passages incorporating shared and commercial functions – in stark contrast  
to the majority of Chinese residential estates, which often seem to strictly  
follow the Modernist model of the functionally segregated satellite city.  
28  
ARC Studio\: Pinnacle@Duxton, Singapore, 2012  
The project was developed as an innovative model for high-density public  
housing. The high-rise conglomeration is located in Singapore’s Chinatown  
and is comprised of seven 50-story towers in a row, which are not only  
linked on the ground floor, but also by imposing sky bridges incorporating  
shared park-like arteries with playgrounds, a jogging path and other  
recreational facilities on the 26th and roof levels.  
41 Oliver Heckmann  
# The Path toward Access and Circulation  
Access describes the path from the public to the private  
sphere and the space it occupies, which begins with two  
thresholds\: the first is the transition into the building; the  
second leads into one’s own apartment. In between lies an  
entire sequence of spaces with horizontal and vertical con-  
nections, which can present a generous face to the public  
sphere, and either inviting or inhospitable in nature, may  
promote or discourage encounters among neighbors. In con-  
junction with open spaces such as balconies and terraces,  
the access also represents connection to the outside – it is,  
one might say, the prelude to a building. Similarly, it is a filter  
that controls and manages intimacy and allows for highly  
diverse lifestyles in close proximity to one another.  
The word “access” is potentially ambivalent. To begin  
with, it simply describes an architectural space that allows  
residents to enter their private sphere; beyond architecture,  
however, it also describes a kind of taking stock, a step-by-  
step build-up of understanding and acquiring insight into  
complex contexts. According to the “promenade architectu-  
rale” postulated by Le Corbusier, which provides the basis for  
exemplary designs such as the Villa Savoye in Poissy \(1929\),  
the entire configuration of the design is derived from the  
path into and through the house. To the user, the “promena-  
de architecturale” is revealed as a scenic sequence of spatial  
perceptions; however, it is also an element that establishes  
a hierarchy, a kind of unwritten user manual with which the  
structure of the house unfolds and becomes accessible to  
the user by virtue of his movement – step by step.  
The Question of Function  
But what is the function of this space if one thinks beyond  
its pure circulation role and wishes to explore its potential?  
The access space is a spatial and social buffer between a  
complex, anonymous public space and the intimate, indivi-  
dual environments of the residents. Sociologists have coined  
the term living environment for such spaces – an area that  
surrounds the residence, protects it while still being a part  
of it and at the same time providing an additional sphere of  
activity for the residents.  
To regard the access space as no more than a thoroughfare  
is to misjudge its potential. After all, it offers ideal conditions  
for neighborly contact, for in contrast to the public space  
the number of local residents is manageable – an important  
prerequisite for people’s willingness to socialize. Moreover,  
encounters take place out of necessity, as it were, and repeatedly;  
neighborly routines can therefore develop and become estab-  
lished. For children, especially, the access space also functions  
as an open space – located beyond the boundaries of the  
parental apartment but still sheltered. Thus it becomes part  
of their living environment in a most natural fashion – and it  
becomes a space in which they linger.  
The question whether the access space is merely a passage  
space or whether one also enjoys spending time in it, is first  
and foremost in the hands of the residents. They might be  
able to live in the building without the access space as a social  
interface; however, when the space invites this type of use by  
virtue of its architecture and program, it is readily utilized as  
such. The invitation to linger is thus an offer with no strings at-  
tached. If it is accepted, the access space can become a place  
… Inside\: we enter, we walk around and the forms take on meaning, they expand,  
they combine with one another. Outside\: we approach, we see, our interest is  
aroused, we stop, we appreciate, we turn around, we discover. We receive a series of  
sensory shocks, one after the other, varying in emotion. … \[And\] the ‘jeu’ comes into  
play. We walk, we turn, we never stop moving or turning towards things … Note the  
tools we use to perceive architecture\: we have two eyes that can only look forward,  
we can move our heads from side to side and from top all the way to the bottom,  
we can turn our bodies or transport our bodies on our legs, all the while turning.  
The architectural sensation we experience stems from hundreds of different  
perceptions. It is the ‘promenade’, the movements we make that act as motor for  
architectural events.  
Le Corbusier, cited in\: Danièle Pauly\: Le Corbusier\: The Chapel at Ronchamp, 2008  
In the film “Mon Oncle” by Jacques Tati \(1958\), the path is staged of a resident through an old Parisian  
apartment building leading up the stairs to his attic loft. In this case, the pathway space is no longer  
recognizable as a monofunctional access space with clear boundaries, but is entirely interwoven with the  
structure of the building, thus negating a clear hierarchy and separation between access space and  
accessed space. The viewer almost has the impression that the path to his apartment leads through all  
other apartments in the building; the access space thus gains an openness and boundlessness, as if all  
living areas were overlapping.  
42 The Path toward Access and Circulation | Oliver Heckmann  
Guillermo Vázquez Consuegra\: Calle Ramón y Cajal, Seville, 1987  
Gallery with benches Ludwig Mies van der Rohe\: Lake Shore Drive Apartments, Chicago, 1951  
Ground floor with lobby  
Heinrich Tessenow\: Hellerau Garden City, 1911  
Row house with wooden bench  
for casual meeting, communal identification, and representa-  
tion for all residents.  
On the other hand, the access space is invariably in a  
tenuous relationship with the rest of the building, because  
efficiency dictates that it should occupy as little area as possible.  
This is an economic dilemma, especially evident in social  
housing developments where the dictum of economic efficiency  
has resulted in access spaces that are spatially, functionally, and  
qualitatively impoverished. Often, too many units are attached  
to a single access system. As a result, the access system beco-  
mes anonymous and inhospitable and is one of the reasons for  
the social problems arising in buildings of this kind.  
Forms of Access and Circulation  
Shaping the circulation in terms of space and program is one  
of the great challenges in housing. In this context, how to  
deal with the access space also depends on how habita-  
tion is understood and on the question of where habitation  
begins\: in front of the building, behind the front entrance or  
only behind the apartment door?  
Depending on the urban context and the building type,  
the access spaces vary in expanse and complexity\: in the case  
of detached houses and larger housing estates, the houses  
are often surrounded by additional buildings, green spaces or  
common areas – and in some cases shared spaces within the  
buildings – which taken together can be regarded as part of  
this space and its potential. Apartment towers often feature  
lobbies in the entrance area, which deliberately delay the  
transition into the private sphere and invite residents to linger  
for a while. The access space tends to be more compact in  
urban infill developments, where details can play a key role\:  
whether there is a canopy or not, whether the entrance door  
is recessed and thus sheltered from the elements, how the  
threshold is designed, how spacious, bright and inviting the  
space on the inside of the front door is, whether the landings  
are large enough to encourage social interaction, or even  
whether there are opportunities along the way to sit down for  
a brief chat.  
Direct Access from Street or Courtyard  
If apartments are directly connected with either the street  
or a courtyard, each resident has the privilege of having a  
personal entrance. Row houses, for instance, where multiple  
units have direct street access, provide each resident with  
an individual street address, often with a front garden or at  
the very least a front step. Tessenow articulates this area in a  
very subtle manner – running along multiple house fronts is  
a continuous stoop with room enough at the top for a bench.  
The space belongs to the individual unit and acts as a buffer  
despite being in a public setting – ideal circumstances for  
pleasant yet sheltered neighborly interaction.  
In a courtyard, individual units are accessed from a semi-  
public open space either enclosed on all sides or part of an  
open building ensemble. This spatial arrangement forms a  
safe space for communal activities or events, which in turn  
may aid in community development.  
Darbourne & Darke\: Marquess Road, London, 1976  
Burkhard Meyer\: Falken residential and commercial building, Baden, 2006  
In this project, residential units are stacked on top of commercial spaces.  
The entrances to the duplex units are lined up around an office atrium’s  
skylight in the middle of the courtyard – a tranquil and quiet space for  
interaction.  
43 zanderroth architekten, BIGyard, Berlin, 2010, section  
Townhouse \(street access\) & garden house \(courtyard access\)  
Henri Ciriani\: Morgenstond, Dedemsvaartweg,  
The Hague, 1994  
Four units per floor in open atrium, entries to

Kaden Klingbeil Architekten\: e3, Berlin, 2008  
Access to apartments via footbridges and  
recessed open spaces  
In the project BIGyard, a townhouse typology forms part  
of a block edge development providing each unit with its own  
entrance and a room for potential commercial use despite  
its inner-city location. The garden houses run along the rear  
firewall and are accessed from the shared courtyard\: eat-in  
kitchens form a buffer and threshold between the two.  
Stairs  
A set of stairs is probably the smallest common denomina-  
tor among all residential building access systems. To begin  
with stairs are a mundane, functional building component;  
however, they also serve to stage the passage into the house.  
In baroque architecture, stairs evolved into complex, three-  
dimensional spatial configurations and became the stage  
for social encounters. The path towards the destination was  
artificially prolonged in order to endow the event of the en-  
counter with time and space. In ordinary housing, stairwells  
can be designed in such a fashion as to consciously shape  
and publicly present the connection of residents with their  
environment.  
In Álvaro Siza’s housing development “Full Stop and  
Comma” in The Hague, the stairs define the character of the  
building to a large degree\: access is via a portico – a wide  
set of stairs leads directly from the street to a loggia-like  
space, which is open towards the cityscape and from which  
all apartments are accessible through front doors exclusive  
to each unit.  
Vertical Access Systems  
The most commonly used stairwell in housing consists of  
several stacked flights of stairs that give access to a specific  
number of apartments at each landing\: thus there are stairs  
that provide access to a single unit, two, three, and more  
units per floor. Horizontal access systems are preferred for  
buildings in cases where the number of units per level is too  
high. There are numerous subcategories to these models;  
there are buildings with internal or external stairs, which  
are combined with short horizontal corridors or housed in  
a separate building section. When they are constructed as a  
shaft, vertical access systems of this type can contribute to  
the structural integrity of a building and increase the design  
flexibility within the apartments.  
The ten-story residential building by Henri Ciriani in The  
Hague demonstrates the possibilities of variation for this  
type. The apartments are organized into four units per floor;  
what is remarkable in this case is how the space around the  
sets of stairs expands into an open atrium reaching across all  
floors between the two building wings. On each floor, bridges  
connect to the landings and lead to the loggias projecting into  
the atrium, and it is these loggias that serve as entrance areas  
for the units. Beyond the loggias, the atrium opens onto the  
city, while the comings and goings of the residents become an  
event in its interior.  
The same access type is interpreted in an entirely different  
manner in the project e3 by Kaden Klingbeil\: for reasons of  
fire safety, the stairwell lies in a building component set apart  
from the apartment units. Because the building is comprised  
of a wooden structure, the escape route had to be constructed  
in reinforced concrete and set apart from the main building.  
The open gap gives each apartment a third facade and allows  
for more flexible interior spaces, thanks to the separation of  
Álvaro Siza\: “Full Stop and Comma,” Schilderswijk, The  
Hague, 1988  
Raised ground floor with six separate front doors  
Rem Koolhaas refers to a “simple cartoon, … which describes the ideal  
performance of a skyscraper,” with the “skyscraper as a utopian instrument  
for the unlimited acquisition of new territory on a single urban site.” In this  
cartoon, “a slender steel construction … \[supports\] 84 horizontal levels, all  
of which are identical in size to the original lot. Each of these artificial  
levels is treated as a virgin piece of land – as if the other levels did not  
exist – in order to erect a self-contained empire with a single manor as  
focal point …” \(Rem Koolhaas, Delirious New York, 1978/1999\).  
For Koolhaas, this drawing is synonymous with a radical trend toward  
verticality in building, made necessary by the tremendous economic  
pressure on building lots in Manhattan, and made possible by innovative  
skeleton construction.  
In 1853, Elisha Otis installed a spring-loaded brake, which would prevent  
the elevator cabin from falling and crashing in the event of a cable  
rupture. Due to the high crash risk, these vertical transport machines were  
at that time usually reserved for applications in mining and industry. The  
safety mechanism meant that access cores could reach previously  
unattainable building heights, reducing stairs to secondary escape routes  
and allowing for tremendous efficiency in stacking levels.  
In 1854, Otis demonstrated the safety of his braking mechanism at the  
Crystal Palace World‘s Fair exposition in New York City.  
44 The Path toward Access and Circulation | Oliver Heckmann  
Schipporeit & Heinrich\: Lake Point Tower, Chicago, 1965 A. + P. Smithson\: Robin Hood Gardens, London, 1972 – “Streets in the Air”  
the stairwell. Moreover, the access via a footbridge is pushed  
into the urban space and thus emphasized. On three fl oors,  
the apartments are accessed via generous, recessed open  
spaces, which enhances the sense of possessing one’s own  
fl oor and home in the city.  
Circulation as Separation –  
The Invention of the Circulation Core  
In the enclosed circulation cores of larger and higher  
buildings, vertical access systems are taken to the extreme\:  
effective skeleton structures in steel or reinforced concrete  
and elevator facilities allow for extremely tall residential  
buildings with a steady repetition of the building’s hori-  
zontal footprint. It is the use of elevators in access cores  
that radically reduces the amount of time that residents  
spend in this common interface. In this sense, the vertical  
circulation core is the symbol of an autonomous treatment  
of each fl oor and its inhabitants. The fl oors inhabited by  
“others” are merely perceived in a fl eeting glimpse through  
the opening and closing elevator door; residents disembark  
only on their own fl oor.  
There are, however, exceptions to the rule. At Citygate  
Tower in Vienna, a vertical incision in the building provides na-  
tural lighting to the central circulation core, opening it up to a  
view of the city. Additionally, shared and in part double-height  
spaces are inserted into this cut-out void every few fl oors and  
are used for instance as a media room, home theater, laundry  
room, play area or roof terrace.  
Horizontal Connecting Spaces – “Rue Intérieure” and  
“Streets in the Air”  
Central corridors such as the “Rue Intérieure” and external  
access areas such as galleries or “Streets in the Air” are  
horizontal access systems.  
Corridor  
A corridor is a horizontal passage located in a building’s inte-  
rior, which for the most part runs parallel with the building’s  
facade and is lined on both sides by residential units. A  
groundbreaking example of this type of access system is the  
Unité d’Habitation, which emerged as a result of research on  
urban living. Le Corbusier established that it would require a  
minimum of 1,600 residents to create a functioning piece of  
urbanity and it was from this assumption that he derived the  
scale of the Unité – complete with retail strips, communal  
establishments and the so-called “Rues Intérieures.” Located  
on every third fl oor, they provide access to the left and right  
to maisonette units, which occupy the entire width of the  
building on the fl oors above or below. In contrast to the  
galleries, these interior streets have virtually no relationship  
with the surroundings\: running the entire length of the  
building, dark and with low ceilings, they failed to achieve  
the effect of a lively streetscape.  
Van den Broek and Bakema’s residential high-rise in  
Berlin also features an internal street. However, in this case  
it receives natural light from two sides and opens onto a  
two-story shared loggia on the south side with sun decks  
and playground terraces. The number of units along each  
corridor is manageable in this case. Spatially and program-  
matically, the access area is thus intentionally designed as a  
communal area.  
Le Corbusier\: Unité d‘Habitation, Marseille, 1947 – “Rue intérieure”  
Van den Broek, Bakema\: residential tower Hansaviertel, Berlin, 1958  
Shared loggia at the southern end of internal streets  
querkraft\: Citygate Tower, Vienna, 2015
